Medical.
Dr, RICHARDS.
The new ELECTRICAL, CONSULTING and
LABORATORY ROOMS being completed, Dr.
Richards will in future be pleased to welcome hia
friends and patients, during his consulting hours,
at his new address,
3A COLLEGE STREET, SYDNEY.
This eminent Specialist, Founder, and Chief
Surgeon of the long-established and favourably-
known Dispensary that now bears his name, re-
ceived his medical education in the leading college?
and hospitals of Europe. He has devoted a life-
time to the study of
PRIVATE, NERVOUS, AND SKIN
DISEASES.
He has by profound research thoroughly investi-
gated their origin, traced their deadly sources and
insidious dissemination, and ascertained the surest
and safest methods by which they may be eradi-
cated, with these widely recognised qualifications
combined with unswerving faithfulness to his
patients, he has during his practice, extending
over FORTY YEARS, attained a well-deserved
pre-eminence in their treatment. His practice
embraces diseases which probably cause more un-
happiness than any other class of troubles that
afflict mankind.
YOUNG MEN
who suffer from the results of early follies, trans-
gressions or excesses of more nature ago, bladder
difficulties, a whitish or dark ropy sediment in
the water, accompanied by slight burning or
smarting sensation, Headaches, Drowsiness during
the day, circles round the eyes, Giddiness,
irregularity of the bowels, specks before the eyes,
bashfulness, discontentedness, weakness or pains
in the back. Liver or kidney complaints, dreams,
buzzing noises in the ears and head, pimples, a
fear that something dreadful is about to happen,
weak stomach, yellow or blood-shot eyes, impure
blood, hasty, uncontrollable temper, hacking
cough, who cannot marry, nervousness in society,
wakefulness, palpitation of the heart, general

SATURDAY, APRIL 9, 1892.
The “Prevailing Depression.”
Last week Governor Jersey dismissed the
New South Wales legislators to rest from
their labours, and in his valedictory speech,
among other statements of a more or less
original character, he cast a regretful glance
at the “depression which lias recently
afflicted Australia in common with the rest
of the civilised world.” The same allusion,
in one form or another, turns up in the
speech of every other Governor in the Aus-
tralasian provinces, and of almost every
potentate throughout the whole civilised
earth. It has appeared with a weary itera-
tion for many years —so many, in fact, that
nobody now living can remember when the
depression first began. Yet even now it is
the fashion to allude to it as a mere
temporary phase—a passing circumstance
which, in the ordinary course of events, must
provide its own remedy, and to assume that
prosperity is the normal condition of society,
and depression only an excrescence upon the
surface. Therefore, there is a constant
desire to supply a reason for the depression,
and in Maoriland it is described by the Tory
press as a regretable phenomenon which
arises from the conduct of the Ball an ce
Government in “driving capital out of the
country ; ” and in New South Wales the
eternal W. E. Abbott writes to the daily
papers to point out that the existing stagna-
tion is entirely owing to strikes and sinful
combinations among the proletariat, and the
equally eternal Epps explains that the way
to salvation lies in establishing the system
of a “ vote for Thrift.” In Queens-
land, again, the Griffilwraith Cabinet
announces that the absence of Kanaka
labour is the sole cause of the trouble, and
throughout Australia in general there is a
further hazy and undetinable impression
that the want of more public works and the
falhug-ofl' in the “ influx of foreign capital ”
are in some way responsible for everything.
Meanwhile in Europe the widespread woo is
ascribed to too much “ congestion ” in large
cities, and in America it is set down to in-
dustrial troubles, and in other parts of this
depressed globe it is charged to a variety of
local causes. Everywhere the same assump-
tion is present, that prosperity is the natural
and reasonable state of things as society is
now organised, and that “ commercial de-
pression ” is a derangement of the course of
nature and a departure from orthodox con-
ditions generally.
* * %
Therefore the poor all over the world are
urged to wait in patience till the depression
passes away, after which a commercial mil-
lennium will start and all will be well. It is
a shadoivy and impersonal thing, that pro-
vailing stagnation—like the devil, and the
“act of God,” and similar influences it is
always out of reach ; and so long as the world
believes in it much trouble is saved. But
what hardly any one seems to realise is that
the depression is a permanent institution—
that it is the rule while prosperity is the
exception—in other words, that there is no
genuine depression at all, for the term signi-
fies that things are below their customary N
level, whereas in reality they are merely on
their natural basis, and any rise above that
point is not a return of prosperity, but only
a sudden financial drunkenness. The world, in
fact, is in its normal condition—the condition
which it will occupy till the end of time, unless
land-monopoly and free competition and the
other evils of the present social system are
abolished, and humanity starts on an entirely
new basis. The “ unemployed question,”
and the “submerged tenth,” the uni-
versal famine, and the eternally recurring
problem of relief works, the soup-kitchens
and the industrial troubles and the shortage
in the national accounts, and the perpetual
demand for borrowed money to “ absorb
surplus labour ” and all the rest of the world’s
difficulties are not owing to any temporary
falling-off in trade and industry; they arc part
of its very existence, and to wait for an
“industrial revival” as a remedy for these
conundrums is as hopeless and purposeless as
to wait for the resurrection. The return of
prosperity and the event which Micawber
vainly hoped might turn up are equally
shadowy, and, moreover, neither of them
ever come. It is assumed, in an indefinite
sort of way, that there has been and, in the
ordinary course of things, will be again, if
people only wait long enough, a time when
every industrious man can find work and
food, but this assumption is altogether base-
less. The ordinary course of things, as
society is now constituted, is that from one-
tenth to one-half of the human race shall
live perpetually on the verge of starvation.
It is mere waste of words to call this state of
affairs “ depression.” The world is always
depressed, and the only variety it affords is
that some parts are more depressed than
others.
# # *
The effort to prove that extreme poverty
is a mere accident arising from temporary
local causes is an inevitable failure. The
troubles of Maoriland, as already mentioned,
are ascribed by the monopolist press to the
alleged fact that the policy of the Ballanoe
Cabinet has “driven capital from the
country,” but even assuming this to be true
the capital must have gone somewhere, and
the place that it has gone to shows no visible
signs of improvement. The woes of Queens-
land are said to be owing in great part to the
want of population to develope the sugar
industry and the other resources of the
country ; and by an upside-down process of
reasoning those of the poorer classes in Eng-
land are said to be owing to the presence of
too much population, and this population is
declared to be starving for want of land to
settle upon, and meanwhile more than one-
third of the arable lands in Great Britain are
lying waste and tenantless for want of any-
one to settle upon them. The bankruptcy
of the middle classes in the same distressful
land is entirely owing to the fact that they
rashly lent some £300,000,000 to sundry
defaulting South American states, and are
unable to get it back, but the states which
have absorbed all this money are also bank-
rupt, so by this reasoning the people who
have the money and the people who do not
have it come equally to grief. The difficulties
of ’the United States arise from industrial
agitation and the low price of grain, and
those of Russia from the high price of grain,
and the fact that the industrial classes dare
not agitate lest they should be sent to
Siberia. The poverty of the South African
provinces is owing to the falling-qft' in
the diamond-fields, and this is held
to be sufficient to account for the
fact that they are poorer now than many
other countries which never possessed any
diamond-fields at all. The Northern Ter-
ritory is languishing because it is short of
Chinamen and coolies, and India and China
are both in difficulties because they are not
short of Chinamen and coolies. And over
and above these alleged local causes, they are
infected by the temporary depression of
their neighbours, and no one seems to realise
that, with a very few exceptions, the world
has always been depressed since the begin-
ning of history, and bids fair to keep on
being depressed to the end.
* * *
Almost every time the human race has
quitted the safe, but dismal, path of depres-
sion it has come to worse grief as the im-
mediate consequence. 'I he Victorian land-
boom was a period of sudden affluence, for
then nearly everybody exchanged his gold
for debentures or other alleged securities,
and the man who had the gold, and the man
who had the debentures, both reckoned
them as solid assets, and the national wealth
was much enhanced thereby. The deben-
tures are mostly gone by this time, and as
the natural consequence the old, familiar
depression has returned. The period of the
railway mania in England was also an out-
burst of prosperity, and great was the fall
thereof. The last outbreak of affluence in
Europe was the era of foreign loans, when
for a brief period all the world was com-
paratively prosperous ; the borrowers
because they had the cash, and the
lenders because they had the paper securi-
ties and imagined that they would have
the interest. Now, both parties are
practically insolvent; the temporary delirium
which is called progress is over, and the
orthodox, sane condition, which is defined
as commercial stagnation, has returned once
more. There is no possibility of accounting
for this condition upon the hackneyed theory
of the departure of capital, for all the capital
the earth ever possessed is still somewhere
upon its surface, and it cannot depart unless
it flies .off into space. Neither can it be
ascribed to “ industrial agitation,” according
to the favourite manner of the Conservative
press ; despite the Queensland shearers
strike, for instance, the annual wool-crop
was despatched as usual, and the money
came back in the same old way, and the
total wealth of the province was not
diminished one iota. Moreover, the uni-
versal character of the existing poverty is a
sufficient proof that it arises from no mere
temporary or local influences. With the
single exception of France, and in a less
degree the United States, which may be
termed prosperous because they are much
less unprosperous than the average, there is
hardly a spot upon the entire globe which is
not suffering, more or less, from the same
trouble. Even the fierce competition of
modern industrialism is an insufficient ex-
planation ; if the world’s difficulties all arose
from competition, then there must be some-
where, at least, one successful competitor to
be found —the one who has come out on top.
It is difficult to realise a competitive system
in which everybody is undermost —a battle
in which both parties are defeated, and
nobody ever wins.
# • *
Ever since the first newspaper first existed
the Journalist of the period has alluded feel-
ingly to this same old evil, and has expressed
the same old hope that it would presently
pass away, and be succeeded by a period of
uninterrupted prosperity. He might as
well express a hope that Death would
presently die out, or that disease would
become extinct, or that eternity would fall
down dead and be no more heard of. The
depression goes on for ever, and even in its
prosperous periods the world is still depressed
—only being drunk and irresponsible it
isn’t aware of the fact. As long as land-
lordism exists the human race is burdened
with more liabilities than it can pay,
and remains poor as a consequence ;
it is possible, under a reasonably en-
lightened system, for every denizen of this
planet to support himself upon it, but when,
in addition, he has to pay x’ent for the planet
the load becomes too great for endurance.
In France, where the landlord is more nearly
abolished than in any other civilised land,
there may be found the nearest approach to
absolute prosperity that the world has yet
seen—but for the load of militarism the
France of to-day would be an earthly Para-
dise. In England, where landlordism is
seen in its worst and most offensive form,
and the burden of Freetrade is added to the
burden of rent, the national poverty is most
abject and most incurable. The Anglo-Saxon
is the Micawber of the human race ; he is
always waiting for something to turn up.
Though the present commercial stagnation
began about the period of the Heptarchy, he
still persuades himself that it is only a pass-
ing difficulty—an altogether unforeseen em-
barrassment somewhat akin to catarrh or
cold feet or any other minor evil to which
the human race is liable. Therefore he is
always floating a new loan in order to start
some fresh public works, and thus “absorb
surplus labour” until the financial clouds
roll by, or doing something of an equally
intelligent character. And he never by any
chance appears to realise that he might as
well wait till the Almighty Himself rolls by,
and that, while the present social system
lasts, the real meaning of the situation is
that the country is making a vain attempt to
stave things off till the millennium.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
A New Financial Device.
Dear Bulletin, — At the present time, when
the old debentures floated some *25 or 30 years
ago arc steadily falling due, and the money
Avith which to repay them is a non-existent
quantity, and the British lender is practically
insolvent through the blind, unreasoning faith
with which he lent his money to Argentina and
other broken communities, and new loans are
unobtainable in England, Avhile even the con-
A r ersion of old ones is a matter of exceeding
difficulty, the great question of the day is lioav
to dispense Avith the entire race of London
financiers, and raise Avhat money is absolutely
needed in Australasia itself. Under these cir-
cumstances, I would suggest that every bank,
insurance company, and other joint-stock
financial institution doing business in' these
provinces Avhich lias, or professes to have, a
reserve fund, should be required to invest (say)
one-half of t hat fund in (government securities,
and deposit the said securities in the Supreme
Court as a guarantee that they really exist, and
have not been sold, pavvned, mortgaged, em-
bezzled, or otherwise made away with. These
bonds could bear interest, if necessary, at 4fj
per cent.; the chances are that, allowing for
discount, commission, brokerage, and the
enhanced rates iioav demanded in the British
money-market, Australia Avill soon have to pay
that amount to the English loan-monger in any
case. I do not suggest this as a means of
creating neAv liabilities—there are more than
enough in existence already ; but as a device,
whereby to raise money to pay off the old ones,
the plan lias sundry advantages. The principal
of th esc is that it Avill afford a reasonable
amount of protection to shareholders and de-
positors against their enemy the director, and
his brother in iniquity, Ananias, the auditor.
When these individuals show—as they invari-
ably do —a huge reserve on paper, they are
never called upon to produce any evidence that
it exists anywhere else than on paper; the
shareholders and creditors have merely the
assurance of the hired Ananias and his em-
ployer to that effect. When the explosion
comes, it generally proves that the alleged
reserve consists of dead men’s bills and the
I O U’s of men who never lived, of P.N.s signed
by miscellaneous bankrupts, of unsecured debts
owing by persons who , have quitted the
country with the police in their wake, and of
similar “ assets.” If it were provided bylaw
that 50 per cent, of the reserve, which every
joint-stock company shows in its balance-sheet,
should be invested in national securities in the
manner indicated, it would then become a
matter of certainty that half the alleged accu-
mulations, at all events, could be found when
wanted, and that would be about as much as
could reasonably be expected. Iso sane share-
holder or depositor ever hopes to find the whole
of a reserve fund ; the trouble, as things stand
at present, is that ho hardly ever finds any
of it. Then the company which could not
produce its alleged cash would have to acknow-
ledge openly that it had no reserve at all, and
would no longer be able to keep up a fictitious
aspect of prosperity, and to rook the public on
the strength thereof, by passing oft' its dis-
honoured cheques, and other waste-paper and
ancient rags, as accumulated profits. And if,
at the same time, the Australian Governments
would adopt the device—now practised in Eng-
land —of requiring all funds held in trust under
wills to be invested in Government bonds,
except when otherwise directed by the testator,
another convenient method of raising money
would be developed and—provided the bonds
were deposited in the Supreme Court—the
orphan would find his patrimony intact when he
cime of age a great deal oftener than he does at
present. These two schemes would benefit the
Government much and would benefit the public
still more. They arc urgently wanted, even if
the State made no profit out of them whatso-
ever, and the fact that they promise to fill a
woeful gap in the financial system is an additional
reason why they should be tried without delay.
—Yours, &c., Titus Salt.
A “ Cowardly Sentiment.”
The S.M. Herald, the organ of everybody
who is connected, no matter how distantly,
with the “ aristocracy,” publishes a bitter wail
because Home Secretary Matthews has finally
decided that Mrs. Osborne, the pearl-thief and
enterprising perjuress, must serve out her sen-
tence of nine months’gaol -which sentence, but
for her good looks and “social position,” might
easily have been seven to fourteen years. The
following is the most wailful part of the
Herald’s story -
There is so much cowardly sentiment indulged on such
questions that it is a daring tiling perhaps to say that the
penalty exacted from Mrs. Osborne in the outward form
of imprisonment should not be the same as that which
should be exacted from a charwoman. No doult she
deserved severe punishment. She committed an offence
not only against friendship and honour and honesty, but
an offence against the law, of the most dangerous kind ;
for of all crimes probably perjury is that which strikes
most heavily against the foundations of justice, and tends
most to shake the stability of social life. Admitting even
that the period of imprisonment imposed is much shorter
than that usually attached to such a serious crime, it is
possible to conceive that the punishment which she has
been called to endure is more than would be involved in
three years imprisonment to the charwoman. Indeed, the
mere imprisonment is but a small portion of the suffering
inflicted, if it has not been even a relief to her to he
separated for a time from the world. The humiliation of
the conviction, the social ruin of her life, the disgrace
and the suffering which she has brought on some
very dear to her, must, to a refined and
sensitive gentlewoman, be as painful as physical
torture at the stake ; and from these the typical char-
woman, if we can imagine such a one, would be almost
entirely free. Simple conviction and immediate pardon
would have involved punishment to Mrs. Osborne incom-
parably greater than years of hard labour at the tub in a
prison-yard would be to the charwoman ; so that the talk
of even-handed justice in giving the same period of im-
prisonment to each is simply nonsense. We would not
wallop a rhinoceros and a high-spirited horse with the
same stick even for the same offending ; and to give to a
gentle and sensitive lad the same thrashing that we would
delight in giving to a hoodlum would n6t be even-handed
justice. Mrs. Osborne deserved to suffer, and she has
suffered. But the feeling that rejoices in the exaction of
the full measure of her penalty is not a just one, but a
dishonourable pandering to prevalent sentiments that are
falsely supposed to be democratic.
It is difficult to imagine anything' more
utterly callous in its dull, hog-like bruta-
lity than the Herald when it gets into
one of its humane moods. In the present
instance, it starts out cheerfully with the as-
sumption that a thief and utterly brazen per-
juress, who has the command of money and
moves in “society,” must necessarily bo a
“ refined and sensitive gentlewoman ” ; while a
charwoman, though she might be scrupulously
honest and truthful, is necessarily coarse, un-
feeling, brutal, hardened, and altogether an
eminently suitable person to do “ years of hard
labour at a tub in a prison-yard ” should the
law once get hold of her. The thieving and the
perjury and the other efforts which the prisoner
made to prove that she was neither refined
nor sensitive, make no difference ; she
belongs to “society,” therefore she must
be full of delicate feelings, even though
she doesn’t know it. 1)e Cobain, who
is lying low on the Continent to escape
trial for an altogether unprintable offence, also
belongs to society ; so do the Clcvcland-street
gang, and many more, and the same logic
applies to them in equal measure. The
Herald’s theory, iu short, is that every person
who has money and position is sensitive and
refined, and every person who has no money
is low and coarse and debased. If Mrs.
Osborne were to be swindled out of her pro-
perty, then she might become a charwoman
from necessity, and having become one the
Herald would promptly demand that she
should expiate her offence by putting in
five years at the wash-tub. The simile
of the race - horse and the rhinoceros is
good in its way, but the theory that the race-
horse is invariably the animal with money and
the rhinoceros the animal that is stone-broke is
evidence that the paper is one part pimp and one
part groveller. The Herald and similar prints
always demand that the rich offender should be
gently reproved and set at liberty, on the
ground that he or she is so utterly broken up
by remorse that further punishment would bo


nperfluous, and that the poor offender should
be flogged or hanged or sent to hard labour for
r long period because ho is impervious to
remorse, and can only be reached through the
medium of his hide. This is the assumption
the effects of which make the Hogging system
so hateful to any intelligent democrat. The
Bulletin has often remarked that you always
cee a fustian, and never a broadcloth coat,
beside the triangles in the gaol-yard.
+.
Depression Drivel.
j v Victoria just at present the voices of blatant
politicians may be heard expounding to the
ignorant an unwholesome mixture of excuses
for “ prevailing depression,” and prescribing a
number of insane lotions for putting the country
on its legs. Tory candidates attribute the
scarcity of cash to the “strike,” whilst Radi-
cals more or less sincere, are tolerably unani-
mous in declaring that the “ boom ” was the
root of all evil. Members for remote rural dis-
tricts are safe in denouncing the Trades Hall,
the abolition of which infamous conspiracy,
followed by the Government grant of a new
bridge across the local streamlet, would restore
prosperity to the afflicted province. City and
suburban candidates, on the other hand, are
unduly anxious for a new railway policy, inas-
much as the future of the country is supposed
to be wrapped up in an immediate necessity
for making the railways pay (loud cheering).
Fresh taxation and a reduction in Civil Service
salaries are other favourite devices for pro-
moting the general welfare and dispelling the
cloud that hangs over everybody, but the desire
for these benefits seems to be governed by
purely personal considerations. The Radical
with a large family in small Government billets
generally believes in treating the Civil Servants
fairly, and the totally abstaining Tory recom-
mends the instant repeal of land taxation at
the expense of beer. But on two great points
the whole political mob seems to be agreed.
National prosperity is the inevitable out-
come of “sound legislation,” and a country
with a “ surplus ” must surely thrive.
Three hundred aspirants for election have
decided that “surplus” spells salvation. A
satisfactory Budget is the only guarantee of a
bounteous harvest, a splendid gold crop, a
rapid growth of manufactures, and a general
revival in trade. The bloated middlemen of
Flinders-lane, and the great “ representative ”
non-producers of Collins-street, take up the
parable and gravely protest that the first duty
of the Government is to revive trade. Mel-
bourne at large is keeping its eye on the Trea-
surer, in the vague expectation that he will
pull things through. Meanwhile, the local
press has discovered that hard times will come
again no more, now that the associated banks
have tightened the brazen bonds that bound
them together. “We can confidently hope,”
crows the Standard’s boss boy leader-writer,
“ that the long period of disaster, depression,
and distrust which has existed in financial
circles ended with the conclusion of the
bankers’ meeting on Saturday,” Thank heaven
the bankers’ have decided to save Victoria !
Let us hope they will be able to save them-
selves—and their depositors.
4.
The New South Wales bogus “National”
“ vote for Thrift” Association should be called
the “ Have ” Association. It is the Association
which is arrayed in battle-order against the
“have-nots.” It masquerades as a working-
man’s benefit society, but is a scheme of the
monopolists to barricade their interests against
the rising tide of democracy. It seeks the
assistance of all who have money or rank to
lose; to those whose sole capital is brain and
muscle, it offers nothing. It promises to insure
people in the enjoyment of their savings. Just
think of the immense savings of a married
workman, whose average pay is, say, 325.
weekly, wet and fine, throughout the year, and
the exhaustion of the whole of whose income
often barely suffices for the satisfaction of bare
physical wants! Personal freedom, too, it
offers. Freedom of contract—freedom to the
unassociated labourer to die of hunger or accept
whatever conditions are imposed on him;
freedom to be employed at the lowest rate for
the longest hours. It bids for the support of
the small lot-holder and the avaricious work-
man who practices parsimony, and who, when he
gets three figures in the savings-bank imagines
himself equally menaced with the plutocrat.
I he general council of the National Association
consists of squatters, bankers, ship-owners,
landed proprietors—potentates of every hue.
Read their names, enquire about them, and
you will politically understand them.
Melbourne Daily Telegraph has acted
sensibly in reducing its size to four pages
instead of its former twaddling eight. The
present is a good time to start bright, well-
condensed four-page papers, and run rings round
the difluse gossip-rags that have no higher
object in life than to typify waste breath. Aus-
tralian daily papers have of late years run sadly
to seed. They fill up space with as little dis-
cretion as a corporation driver fills up a quarry-
hole with tipoart-refuse. Columns are devoted
to the vacant chatter of nobodies, and a meet-
ing of parish jackdaws is reported at as great a
length us would need to be devoted to a special
synod of archangels called to discuss the proro-
gation of tiie universe. The reason for this
pitiable flattening-out of reports is that daily
newspapers nowadays are chiefly written by
prosy maunderers who should be grocers’-clerks,
instead of by brainy men who can grip the ideas
” their subjects and condense the same into
|nthy crystals. The biggest of the dailies could
Je advantageously condensed into half their
news space. Life is too short to wade through
square miles of shallows. The motto of most
Rallies seems to be to hire cheap inexperience
that knows nothing of the value of space, that
measures its return for wages by the yard and
not by the quality, and that is enthusiastic
discretion, and proud of its laborious
ignorance.
R«v. J. G. Baton puts his anti-Kanaka appoa*
oi’cibly in places. He points out that, because
be Queensland law had declared that Poly-
nesian labour-recruiting for the Northern pro-
vince must cease by the end of ’9O, “ all the
labour vessels which could sail with licenses
before the last day of 1890 kept returning with
their loads of recruits till far into 1891.” The
planters had put on additional vessels for that
year and for years before, thus getting an extra
supply, and they bound down all comers to
serve for three years.—
Then, seeing that it is only about seven or eight months
since the planters ceased to get additional labour, and
seeing that very many of these Polynesians are now
working on the plantations, closing the traffic cannot
account for the decline of the sugar industry in Queens-
land. It has been declining for many years.
Dr. Raton shows that although the young and
healthy among the islanders were recruited,
“ the mortality amongst them has been very
great, the largest per cent, in the world, by
which many thousands of them have been laid
in their graves in Queensland, buried like dogs,
and others brought so to labour, suffer, and die
by the planters’ greed of gain.” Touching
which greed, he discourses :
On the sugar-plantations by law they are paid fourpence
per day, if they live to get it, for doing work for which
they would have to pay white men from ss. to Bs. per day.
Yet the planters and the Griffilwraithians are
always howling that white men won’t consent
to do the work of the planters. For fourpence
a day ! Let the whole land perish first, and
not alone the sugar industry. Then—
Before me and a number of gentlemen, a planter put
the following questions to a company of his men : “ Are
you happy?” “Do you like plantation work?” “Do
you gtt plenty of food?” “ Would you like to go back
to your islands? ” They replied yes or no, to his delight,
when he said, “ You see how happy they are,” &c. Soon
after I asked them the same questions in their own lan-
guage, when their answers were the reverse of what they
had before given, and they implored me to get them taken
home to Tanna. The traffic is a system of deception and
cruel oppression all through to the islanders.
The insolvency of E. S. Smithurst, tbe
Mudgee newspaper proprietor, who burst up
because the New South Wales Equity Court
granted him three injunctions and a warrant
and wasn’t able to enforce any one of them,
suggests two questions for the Government,
First : Will the State reimburse Mr.
Smithurst’s losses, seeing that it taxes that
gentleman to support the law-courts of New
South Wales under the pretext that these
courts will protect him against injustice—
which they failed to do, and also seeing that
the Court charged Mr. Smithurst a consider-
able amount in fees, &c., and then having taken
his money, it sold him a valueless injunction,
which was just as cheerful for him as though it
had taken his money and then given him a
valueless cheque? Second : Will Attorney-
General Barton devise some new kind of
injunction for future use, which will be a little
more effective than the old one, which latter
document seems only to ruin the plaintiff, while
the defendant drops the Court’s order into the
gutter and jumps on it ?

The Central Division (N.S.W.) squatters want
a 15-years’ extension of their leases in order to
enable them to destroy the rabbits within their
holdings ; besides requiring the Government to
find a £25,000 reward as before, to supply
50,000 tons of wire-netting for jd. an acre
extra rental, and to introduce nominal railway
freights for its carriage. After which, the
rabbits will increase and multiply while the
squatters finance on their improved security,
and await a favourable conjunction of political
parties who will permit freeholds to be
acquired, or will extend leases for another term
of 15 years. Should the Dibbs Government
assent to the proposed wholesale locking-up in
the hands of pastoralists of these valuable
leases, it is safe to say that it will never get a
democratic majority to endorse its action. At
the very least, one-half of the areas should be
made available next year, when the leases
expire, for settlement by small holders. Small
holdings are the solution of the rabbit-question.
The Melbourne Argus, in its last leader on the
One-man-one-vote principle, thuds down what
it evidently regards as an absolutely unanswer-
able crusher, and goes away leaving the demo-
cratic party flattened out. Under this principle,
it says, the English would be driven from India,
for, then, every Hindoo would have a vote, and
he would promptly vote the British race out of
their job as the rulers of the land. Therefore,
the One-man-one-vote idea is altogether wrong,
foolish and impracticable, and the article winds
up with another impassioned declaration that
an extra vote for Property is the correct
thing, and that Thrift ought to be en-
couraged. And it never once occurs to the mole
who writes this screed that the property-vote
system would extinguish the English rule in
India quite as fast as any other; in one case
every Asiatic and every white man would have
one vote, and the Asiatic would wipe the white
man out; in the other the rich white man
would have an extra vote or fifteen extra votes
for Thrift, and the rich Asiatic Would have the
same, and the Asiatic would wipe the English-
man out again. Consequently, if this illustra-
tion proves that manhood suffrage is wrong, it
also proves that the property suffrage is
supremely idiotic. This point, of course,
only proves the Argus’s utter want of reasoning
capacity, for even supposing the property vote
system would enable the English to hold India
and the other system would not, it makes not
the slightest difference to anybody in this
country. It is quite as interesting and quite as
much to the point to mention that under the
One-man-one-vote policy the Eskimo would pro-
bably be unable to hold Greenland—which is
perfectly true, seeing that the elector in that
country would probably be frozen or eaten by a
bear on his way to the poll. To tell the truth,
Australia doesn’t care one solitary copper
whether England holds India or not, or whether
India holds England, or whether they both hold
Mexico. The matter isn’t of the slightest
interest to anybody. United Australia is going
to be a Republic, and One-man-one-vote will be
the electoral basis of that Republic. Let
nobody forget this.

The Unemployed of Sydney.
The night of Ist April, 1892, was a notable
one to the Sydney unemployed : it was the last
on which they occupied the livery-stables wherein
they had found temporary shelter, and it was the
night on which each man for the first time re-
ceived the loan of a Government blanket. At
9.30 only five men were in the big yard—two were
cutting up bread, two preparing the soup. The
two-pound loaf was cut into six cubes ; one of
these, with a half-pint of soup, formed a man’s
supper. The chief cook was a member of the
Soudan Contingent, and he entertained the press-
men with reminiscences, lie stirred the copper
with a pole; two wash-tubs of soup were beside
him.
The men were expected from the meeting at
the Exhibition Building about 10.30. ; mean-
while, the policeman, candle in hand, showed the
visitors over the stables. Several “beds” were
“ planted ” in holes and corners, a bed being a
copy or two of a daily paper. Seven bales of
blankets stood in a loft awaiting the superin-
tendent’s order for unpacking. Five or six men
unable to attend the meeting had already had
their supper and had taken up their couches for the
night. Here upon a bench a coal-lumper was
lying awake. He had no coat. The day’s work
had been his first for six weeks, and after his 12
hours of toil ho had dined on five ounces of bread
and half a pint of soup. In the morning he would
get the same amount of bread, three or four
ounces of cheese, and a cup of “Jersey tea.” To-
morrow would be Saturday-pay-day ; he could
look forward to a coat and a bed.
# * *
One very old man, clad in a ragged shirt
and trousers, was stretched, motionless, upon the
wet stones in one of the horse-stalls. Ills boots
served as a pillow ; his bare feet were scored with
sores and blisters. He was racked by a horrid
cough—the presage of speedy dissolution. A
broad, raised platform ran half-way round the
big yard, but he was too weak to climb upstairs.
He was 72 years old ; he had been sleeping in the
Domain for weeks past, and that “had done for
him.” He was spitting ominously bright blood.
“ We’ll have to take you to the hospital,” said the
policeman, lifting him and bearing him up the
steps. Now he is dead. In a manger a dwarf, a
mere skeleton, seemed to sleep the sleep of death.
He was paralysed on one side, yet his naked arm.
all shrivelled and twisted, looked a perfect mem-
ber of bis wasted body.
» « 3
At one end of the platform a young man
of 22 lay upon bis stomach. “He had been to the
hospital that day; the doctors had removed
eleven boils from his back. The wounds had been
washed and anointed and ‘fixed up ’ with plaster ;
the hospital authorities couldn’t give him indoor
relief.” He had no vest. His back, which might
be that of Job, was half-covered with blood-stained
sticking-plaster. He had only one relative in Syd-
ney—a sister, who was employed as a servant. She
would get her wages on Saturday night; then she
would buy him a shirt. If he would let her, the
good girl would give him every “ bean ” she had.
* # #
“ There is a lot of destitution in Sydney,”
remarked the policeman. “ Whole families sleep
upon the Domain. One woman (a widow) with
two children, used to earn her bread by charing
during the day, and sleep with her youngsters
under the rocks at Mrs. Macquarie’s Chair. One
morning, at two o’clock, a child was born upon
the Domain ; there was nothing to wrap it in, for
a dress was all the clothing the mother had.”
# # 3
Soon the torchmen of the procession re-
turned from the Exhibition building, with the
tidings that blankets would be served out that
night. The soup-vessels—four basins, six cups,
twenty half-pint pots—were ranged upon a little
table. Accordingly, the men on their return
would be let through the gate in detachments of
thirty. When a detachment had finished its
supper, it would go straight away to bed and give
place to a second batch.
• « #
The basins held a pint each and the
nearest men unselfishly took the half-pints or
handed a basin to an old man. In one such case
the receiver said “ you’re a gentleman.” “ Wish
to God I was,” replied the helper.
The cook kept saying, “Help yourselves, men ;
Heaven only helps those that help themselves.”
The soup threatened not to hold out, and one
committee-man, evidently brought up in the milk
industry, suggested adding a bucket of water to
it. Luckily there was enough.
One man, who took no soup, stood apart look-
ing helpless and confused ; when spoken to he
placed his hand upon his mouth and shook
his head. lie was deaf and dumb. “I
am hungry, very,” he . signalled, “ when
will it be my turn?” He was a tailor;
he had had no work for nine weeks. “ I want
work,” he said, “ I am a man—proud—much—so,”
giving one to understand that he wished for no
offer of money. “Would he have a smoke?”
“ Yes.” It was his first pipe for a fortnight.
Another thing he wished for—a quiet place in the
stables to lie down : people did not understand
the deaf-and-dumb.
By 12 o’clock nearly everybody had gone to
rest. Five hundred and fifty men! were huddled
together in every conceivable posture. A half-
dozen were awake, a few just half asleep, moving
restlessly, or rolling from side to side, but the
great majority were looked in the first deep
slumber. A phenomenon was a somnambulist,
unconscious, standing erect in his tattered shirt.
One man, and one only, was drunk. As a punish-
ment he had received no blanket. “ Why had he
spent his money in drink ? ” “I had no money to
spend ; I met a couple of mates ; they shouted,
and two glasses knocks a man over when he’s
weak and hungry.” Only one man begged : he
wanted a pair of trousers.
* • #
The secretary stated that up to date no
scheme for relief work had been propounded. The
committee had been solely occupied in relieving
distress. Four committeemen were engaged in
soliciting subscriptions ; four, provisions ; the two
others and he, the secretary, formed the working-
committee. Ninety-five out of a hundred seemed
well-framed and able-bodied. Almost every pro-
fession and trade was represented ; a great many
were very respectable members of the artisan class;
one third of the total were powerful and earnest
labourers. There were a few coloured men ; not
one Chinaman. Next day they would take up
their quarters in the old Exhibition Building;
there they would be comfortable.
* # #
What became of the female unemployed ?
The secretary had really not thought of that.
Mortgaging Corpses.
A London compositor has just finished a
term of six weeks’ imprisonment, for a baccha-
nalian fraud, lie rushed into his boss’s sanctum
with a woeful visage, and sobbingly stated that
his beloved wife was dead. ilis employer gavu
him =65 wherewith to bury her. He disappeared,
bnt, in an hour or so, returned, more dishevelled
than ever. “My just-born baby’s dead— hoohoo!
—lollered its pore dear ma— uhoo ! —two angels in
heaven— ivahj” The boss advanced him some
wages. The “chapel” of compositors subscribed
for coffin-wreaths. Then he wandered forth sadly
and limply—until round the corner. The funerals
did not happen in due time, and when the wifo
turned up to enquire after her worthy husband, ho
was fairly on the road to gaol.
» • »
Which reminds : There were two bright
spirits connected with a certain Australian weekly
paper—Greely and Day. They paired on one
occasion in a tremendous “bender,” and a day or
two found them both “ broke ” and ready to pawn
their eternal prospects for a pick-me-up. They
consulted deeply and desperately, and they hit
upon a plan, unique and melodramatic. They
rehearsed it firstly in chambers. Then they
pawned a vest for sixpence, went and had a
corpse-reviver apiece, and rehearsed the melo-
drama once more in a pub. parlour, “to make
sure.” Sallied forth Greeley, to the office. He
entered the boss’s den without knocking, with
eyes staring affrightedly out of his head, features
twisted with anguish and body spasmodically
twitching. Placing his right hand upon his fore-
head, while his left shaken and imploringly
in the air, he gasped out with a painful heave:
“My God ! Poo-oor Day !” “ What's he
been doing now, Mr. Greely ?” said the startled
boss. A sigh that seemed to come from a slit
bellows, and then—“ Ah ! he’ll never do anything
any more. He’s dead!" 1 “What?” With a
fearful groan-—“ Dead ! lie came to my apart-
ment this morning, and was talking to me as
cheerfully as ever, when, all of a sudden, he gave
a shudder, and fell down on to my sofa, a corpse !
Imagine the shock to me ! See how my limbs
are quivering !1 ” (So they were, for Greely
had the “jerks” badly.) “Well, what do
you want me to do for you, Mr. Greely ? ” said
the boss, kindly. Greely burst into a passionate
fit of hysterical weeping. “Do for me? (Sol.)
Why, I haven’t a penny in the house. (Tears.)
There’s the police to be informed (lamentation)-,
there’ll have to bean inquest. (A flood of b'ubbery.)
Fancy them cutting up poor old Day ! (Hmvls.)
And I must go and break the news to his poor
wife ! ” (Collapses doicn utterly in a paroxysm
of grief.) “Well,” asked the boss, emotion
half-choking his utterance, “ how much do you
want?” Weepingly: “I must have <£s.” “Here,
then”—writing out a paper—“take this order to
the cashier.” Greely vacated the room, and made
for the cashier. Then he vanished to meet Day.
- . . Meanwhile, Day had been waiting in the
pub. parlour. The one rum had started work in
Ids already demoralised brain. He wanted a
second “straightener” worse than he had wanted
the first. His mind wandered. The minutes
seemed hours to his galloping consciousness.
He lost his ideas. Suddenly, he began
to remember—“Ah! yes; hadn’t he and
Greely made up a plot by which the wind was to
be raised? Of course they had ! What a fool he
was not to have remembered it! lie was to go to
the office and report that Greely was dead. He
recollected it all clearly now. And Greely was
waiting round the corner for him. He would go
at once.” .... The boss was hard at work,
but every now and then paused to mutter : “ Poor
Day! we’ve lost a brilliant man.” The door
opened suddenly. Day entered with a tortured
expression, placed his right hand upon his fore-
head, semaphored his left arm melodramatically,
and hoarsely gurgled :“ My God ! Poor Greely !
He’s dead! He came to my room this morning
and was talk—.” With a bound the boss was out
of his chair. Day flew off at a sudden tangent out
through the door, clean down the front steps into
the street, bewildered, hatless, and thirsting for
Greely’s blood The pair met again
within the next ten minutes; explanations that at
one time were murderously threatening ended in
a fair dividend and a merry carouse.
P.S. —The boss’s name was Traill.

Dear Bulletin,— You asked for a descrip-
tion of the “Tom Collins ” joke. It would take
too much paper to fully describe, but beware of
the man who tells you that “ T.C.” is in the hotel
opposite, talking about a dishonored cheque cf
yours for 255, which he holds. It must have been
played in Sydney years ago, and you have for-
gotten it. If not, there is plenty of fun in store
for the man who starts it. —Dan.
Manager Henry Gyles Turner’s reassuring
circular has put up in price the shares of the Mel-
bourne Bank. There has always been a prejudice
against Manager Turner for his literary proclivi-
ties. Not very long ago he even shocked his
brother bankers by acting Symperson, in Gilbert’s
“Engaged,” at Prahran Town Hall, with distin-
guished amateurs the sort that shine more
on overdrafts than balances. Various defunct
unlucky magazines have wanted to get him as
banker, but he has drawn the line—a little deaf in
that ear. He is prominent at the Unitarian
Church, where his sister Martha, afterwards Mrs.
Webster, was clergywoman.
Three fearless young bank-clerks once
sallied into the Victorian bush bent on capturing
alive or dead the bushranger Power. They were
looking for a roughly-attired man riding on a roan
horse. They met a well dressed civilian mounted
on a dark-bay thoroughbred. Salutations were
exchanged, and, primed with the importance of
there hazardous quest, the bank-clerks talked
about how they were going to divide the Govern-
ment reward between them so soon as they had
netted Power. The civilian turned back a stretch
of the road witli them and wished them luck.
Suddenly as he gained the “ drop” on them, ho
thundered out—“ Bail up, you donkeys; throw
up your arms. I’m Power!” They “bailed.”
Then Power made them one by one strip to their
bare pelts, unsaddle and unbridle their horses,
and burn all their duds and harness. Stampeding
the horses before him, lie bade the naked three
good morning, with some kindly advice as to how
they could mention at the township the her
manner in which they “ caught Power.”

BJC Macquarie-street North, Sydney, April C, 1892.
My Dear Moorabinua, —
The first Sydney' seance of the Australian Home Read-
ing Union, at the Town Hall, the other Monday, loomed
with undeserved importance on the dismal social horizon
of a wet Lenten week, simply because Lady Jersey was
goin’ to fill the chair, and her luvin’ subjects wanted to
see if she had altered much since the remote date on
which they' last saw her. And she was there in a pretty
cosmea-pink silk gown with a darker stripe; the bodice
folded over frontally'; bonnet of ecru lace and pink bows
and strings. She was almost the only' bright spot in a long
wash of frights that surged from the floor to the platform
where sat Lady- Windeyer, Miss Macdonald (the brand-new
lady'-principal of the Ladies’ College, who is revealed to be
a tall savant with non-committal hair and a long pale
face, enhanced on this occasion by a black frock and
hat); Mrs. Wblstenholme; a well-known Judge, who
occasionally inserted his fourth finger in his
mouth; Mr. Goschen in an eye-glass, and several
professors. The crowd was largely' female (though
I had an edifying view of a fortunate young man squeezed
in between two girls who each shared the rim of her chair
with him) and mostly ill-dressed, with one or two excep-
tions. A pretty brunette, in a well-cut black gown, had
a very jaunty hat with bows of red-and-black striped
ribbon ; and somebody else was festive in a scarlet Tudor
cloak softened with black braid—but the rest was rag-bag.
One Australian Home Reader of the gentler sex had her
soiled yellow flounces visibly pinned on here and there ;
and gazing round with the eye of a grandmother at the
swarm of untidy people, one could not help thinking they
wouldn’t find much time for organised reading if they
gave their personal appearance a chance. If the she-mem-
bers of the A.H.R.U. think to justify themselves in the eyes
of the marriageable man by talking his largely-ignorant
head off about all the deep and boring literature they can
lay their hands on, I must rise hastily to submit that the
haggard spinster who discourses lovely Ruskin in undarned
stockings and lank hair is the sort of thing no normal
male exactly yearns to metamorphose into a “ missus.”
As to theobjects(Lady J. pronounces it “objiks”)of the
A.H.R. Union, briefly put, the whole thing seems to be a
deep-laid scheme to heighten the already deplorable
gregariousness of humanity by emphasising what a
beautiful thing it is to read a book and know that thou-
sands of people are reading it at the same time. Certain
courses of study are announced per pamphlet, by the
committee, and the Union has to plough through them or
resign. “ How lovely,” said one speaker in effect, “to
have one’s family all reading the same book ! ” Especially
lovely, I yearned to chip in, when you have just arrived
at the crisis and rush to see how it all ends and find that
somebody else has got the volume. The Union is to have
its moments of relaxation, which are to take the form of
lectures and magic-lanterns without trimmings, and
although there doesn’t appear to be much mental holiday
about a scientific lantern-slide, yet it is quite frivolous
compared to the organised list of brain-worry, which
includes weird and awful tirades on the “Principles of
State Interference,” “ Economics of Industry,” and other
gay and festive tomes calculated to furrow your baby brow
inches deep in a fortnight. But the Union’s courses of
improving literature lean, largely, it is almost unnecessary
to say, towards the side of the Strictly Orthodox.
Can anybody explain the blind passion for slop etceteras
that characterises the Australian woman ? Slop gloves,
slop corsets, slop boots are worn by everybody—from poor
Mary Ann to the woman whom, for want of a better word,
you and I and all of us call “ smart.” And it isn’t because
they have not had chances. An enterprising capitalist
tried to start a kid-glove factory, where gloves could be
made to order, in Sydney once—but where is that factory
now? Melbourne’s best corsetibre not so long ago filed
her schedule, and bowed down to ready-made ; and for
one boot-shop where a genuine follower of St. Crispin
takes -he longitude of your little foot with a measure that
tickles dreadfully, there are a thousand ready-made shoe-
shops, with hoof-cases built to the model of the feet of the
women of the country that exports them. The result is
that the Australian woman suits her waist, feet and hands
to the requirements of somebody else, and loses a lot of
individuality in consequence. Notice the gloved-hand of
any otherwise well-dressed woman. If all the clasps are
hitched, it’s ten to one the fingers hang in little points off
the tips, and if the fingers fit neatly at the top, then they
aren’t divided as deeply as the fingers inside, but join just
below the second joint, thus bringing about the webbed
expression of a duck’s foot. It’s safe to bet that the top
button isn’t fastened when you see a glove like that, and
an unfastened glove spoils the true inwardness of your
get-up, as much as if you hung your bonnet tipsily over
your left ear.
But it’s when she comes to her feet that the indigenous
she (who ought to be particular considering the ineffec-
tive shingles a wise Providence has generally poised her
on) doesn’t know how to look after herself. No wonder
the mincing or sprawling gait of our darlings is a bye-
word among the nations, for all their boots are “slops,”
which the foot is made to fit, and the result is that either
the wearer can’t walk without the moral support of a
gamp, or else her boots wiggle-waggle about on her pedal
extremities as if she were walking on perpetual orange-
peel. And the Australee she goes wrong- even in the
pattern of her bocts, for she generally chooses button-ups
which go pulpy and spread-eagle into a sort of burlesque
of her foot, instead of choosing lace-ups, which keep the
ankles neat from first to last. If, after this little lecture,
you say “Pshaw! our women go properly shod,” just
notice the earthward end of ladies who flip in and out of
carriages with worn-over heels and missing buttons-or
revel in the fall-abroad looking tootsie-wootsies of the
girl who raises her swaying draperies to jump the gutter.
Harping- on the pavement-stampers of my poor, dear
sex, I want to complain of the way in which evening-
shoes are worn about the street. Low-cut dancing-shoes
are unhealthy for out-door wear—they are expensive,
because they are not constructed for uneven surfaces
and, above all, they always look hopelessly vulgar. Yet,
half the girls you fall foul of on the block totter along in
these wooden-heeled, stained-paper abominations, because
they have shallow toes and make the foot look small at
half peep. On the mountain mail-train, the other night,
I saw an obvious bride launch forth on her honeymoon
in a pair of red morocco evening-shoes with brilliant
buckles ! 1 pity the bridegroom when the chilblain season
dawns.
I hear that two or three English “Johnnies” at
Government House, Perth, recently imitated the now
notoriousfrolicsof some well-advertised society girls; they
aimed jellies across the festive board at one another, and
volleyed with tumblers of fizz until the Administration
grew tired of the crash of broken glass and packed the
hilarious bevy off the premises. They have, however,
since been guests at the haunt of the Queen’s representa-
tive.
I want to scold the audience at Sydney Garrick for the
way they applauded Lisa’s first solo on the revival of
“ rjonnambula,” last Saturday. It was, to my mind,
simply hideous—out of tune, wheezing, ear-piercing, yet
when she finished the round of dreary applause struck
up allee same. It may be only courteously done, but
when people shriek rapturously at a vocal failure it makes
genuine applause absolutely meaningless and gives the
singers a fine contempt for the musical discernment in
front. But (he Cutticas were in good voice and sang with
much expression—indeed I have never heard Mr. C. so
vocally wrought up—he seemed to make Elvino’s bothers
his own from curtain-rise. But I must take exception to
his invariable habit—when overcome with stage
grief—of striding to the wings, hugging his head
with his hands and quivering all over like
water jelly-. And-to continue cantankerously-
La Cuttica’s Amina, though vocally bird-like, was one
and the same girl as Gild a, Violet Yatery, Lucia, and so
on, even to the hair, and one can’t help longing for the
lady to borrow the golden wig of the handiest chorus-girl
so as to remove the impression, rapidly gaining ground,
that the operatic Nemesis makes a dead-set at brunettes.
From Cuttica’s clothing in “ Sonnambula” the girl who
wears chemisettes (I mean outwardly) can gain many
hints how to make them look different every time. And
apropos of clothes, it is easy to know just when the
operatic heroine’s lady friends begin to cut her—you can
tell it by her skirt. As the gushing innocent of the
village, it’s touch-and-go betw-een the final flounce and
her knees, but when the inevitable cloud arises, down
comes her petticoat to her ankles in one rush. They do
say sorrow improves people.
One wails “ lohahod ” over Sydney “ Women’s Fund,”
which was such a healthy, bounding institution in Lady
Carington’s time, what with mobbed meetings and the
dailies individualizing the more or less representative
bonnets on the platform, and suburban outsiders running
all over the town for subscriptions, that they might rise
proudly before her ladyship and deposit the shining
shekels at her feet. They wisely called it the “ Women’s
Fund,” in those glad days, and the democratic florin spun
alongside the loyal eighteenpence, and the secretary had
to draw breath twice in announcing the total. But, at
the last meeting of the now-called “Queen’s Jubilee
Fund,” with Lady J. on the poop, a yearly increase of
£7 ss. was timidly revealed to a small and depressed
assembly, which sat in unadvertised gloom and silence.
Which once again makes it apparent that women do
nothing for nothing. The “objecks” of the Fund are
the same as ever—but where are the women who bailed
you up on the Block for charity-subscriptions? You find
the answer in the fact that “live” titled personal
patronage failed the League when Lady Carington went
away.
We may drink too much tea in Australia, but you don’t
utterly condemn the person who is tempted beyond her
strength, do you? I had some tea (Golden Tip Ceylon
Pekoe) sent me recently by Murrell Brothers, 509 George-
street, Sydney, who bought it at auction from Frazer &
Co., and its auction price was £2 17s. per lb.! But the
true tea-worshipper would give twice as much without a
murmur after the first sip. It is like the nectar an angel
might offer you at a Heavenly five o’clocquer.
When lovely woman stoops to writing milk-sop litera
ture for the young, she can hardly be expected to send a
thrill through the grown-up inner consciousness, and so I
approached Mrs. W. Campbell Browne’s little Easter story,
“ The Three Sisters,” with what I should imagine to be
the feeling of an alderman confronted with a mere bun.
It shows very “proper” feeling on the part of Mrs. H-
to sell the famous Hargreaves pearls at auction when they
fetched £lOl6, for (leaving out the fact that their sensa-
tional history was calculated to boom their price) no sen-
sitive woman could wear them with an ex-friend “ doing
time ” in gaol on account of their attractions. By the
way, pearls come next to opals as unlucky gems, and it
would be interesting to see if the Hargreaves’ set of
oysters’ tears bring any misfortune to their new owners,
who must be devoid of all superstition. Pearls just fished
up are looked upon askance by, lots of properly supersti-
tious folks, many of whom wouldn’t take the H. article as
a gift after they had so proved the balefulness of their
influence.
Talking of pearls reminds me of a woman I supped near
to once at a charity ball. She was eating an oyster patO
most deliberately, giving each mouthful more than the
regulation 32 chews, and her cavalier remonstrated that
everything else would be gone before she had finished.
“I alius chew hyster-pattys close,” said she, “for you.
never know but what there’s a pearl in ’em !”
The theatrical libel-case of the moment in Sydney
showed up the bounding opera-troupe lady in a new light
—for the seraph who foots the bill for her own outings-
can’t be such a mercenary charmer as people would fain-
have us think. It transpired that when the ladies of a
certain American burlesque Co. swept forth on horseback,
each of them had the bill for the hire of her prancing
steed deducted from her salary, and each lady paid her
own hoofing as it were. The attendant cavaliers must-
have shone with a very subdued radiance in the eye of the
livery-man. Next time I meet a golden-haired artiste-
perched gingerly on the top of a hired horse, I will regard l
her with the rapt expression that in these bad times is:
certainly due to the person who is recklessly dissipating
twelve-and-sixpence.
Judged in the lurid light of that newspaper libel-case
it would seem that the editorial hubby has sometimes to-
choose between domestic reprisals and the Supreme-
Court. And then comes a monkey damages and all costs.
It must be an awful responsibility to be connected by
marriage with a newspaper !

1 have just learnt that one branch of usefulness prac-
tised by Sydney Y.M.C.A. is to ferret out the stray sheep
whose English parents want to know their whereabouts,
and, when found, to broom the tobacco-ash from their
uncut locks and shake the corks and pawn-tickets out of
their pockets and pack them off home in repentant mood
with their hair neatly parted in the middle. But really
lost-relative finding and forwarding is an ungrateful
industry, because it’s ten to one the family’s anxious
enquiries are only dictated by conscience, and that the
last thing in the world they really want to see is the
prodigal dumping down his Australian blue blanket in
the ancestral hall. He was a gentlemanly-looking prodigal
when they kicked him out, and they always think of him
wandering ’neath a torrid zone with a clean shirt on
everyday, and scent on his handkerchief, so when he
turns up in moleskins that he has worn all the voyage,
and green-hide boots lied with string, he isn’t a bit like
the wanderer of his mother’s dreams (who never smelt of
stale tobacco) and his kith and kin are desperately sorry
they ever roused him out. And the Y.M.C.A. is often
mildly astonished at receiving no thanks for its prompt
delivery of the human consignment—it doesn’t enter into
the family’s murderous feeling towards their helpful
institute when it first became obvious that the restored
picked lus tcetli with his fork.
I want to know if you think it exactly clever of a very
fascinating girl to “do” the various dress-circles with
ma, of whom there is so much, and who waddles so duck-
fully? There always remains the fatal likeness, and you
can t help putting the avoirdupois, and all the rest of it,
on to the beautiful daughter, who carts ma round to
honestly warn her numerous adorers as to what the rose
will turn to by and by. Dear, dear me, it always recalls
in a topsy-turvy way the skull and crossbones legend:
“ As J’oa are, I once was ; as I am, so you will be.”
Yours affectionately,
SAPPHO SMITH.
A paragraph which crept into last issue
concerning the recent marriage of a Hebrew
gentleman of Sydney appears to have caused
much annoyance to those concerned —chiefly by
reason of an allegation to the effect that the
wedding had led to the severance of their connec-
tion with the local Hebrew congregation. As we
are assured that nothing is further from the fact
than this, we have to express regret that any
statement of the kind should have been permitted
to appear.
Directors of Wallaroo (S.A.) mines have
decided that the miners—already the worst-paid
in all Australia—shall in future work under the
open tender [i.c., freedora-of-contract, or “sweat-
ing ”) system.
If a Judge should not adjudicate where his
son appears as counsel, what should be the rule as
to the appearance of Judges’ sons-in-law and
nephews before their papas-in-law and uncles? If
the line is to be drawn at blood-relations, what
about club friendships, Masonic ties, and so on?
The discussion of this question is in full blast at
Brisbane, and the people being discussed are Chief
Justice Lilley and his son “Ned,” It is a terrible
thing to live in a limited community.
Victorian Premier Shiels (a Scotch college
boy) is very young-looking and apparently with-
out “side,” He wears a low-crowned hat and tweed
suit and travels on the tram dummy. As a
barrister he was never successful and as a youth
was thought rather visionary; winning the vice-
Chancellor’s essay-prize at Melbourne University
did him no great good. He first came into
prominence by his Divorce-Bill and his subsequent
trip to England.
The Earl of Huntingdon, whose engage-
ment with Sir Samuel Wilson’s daughter has just
been cabled, though the son of a bankrupt peer,
can claim the bluest of blue blood. He is the
third earl in point of precedence, ranking after
Shrewsbury and Derby. As Macaulay mentions
in his essay on Warren Hastings, the family has
experienced a strange romance. Robin Hood was
the alleged founder of the race. The earl is quite
a young man. His brother is called Warren
Hastings after the Indian nabob.
Old Ebenezer Skinner, bookseller, of
Sydney, is dead. lie fell sick, the other day, and
was told that his heart had given out and that he
bad to die ; for four days thereafter he lay await-
ing the arrival of the scythe-bearer, his one great
anxiety the while being that after he had gone
some clergyman might promulgate a specious
story that in his last hours he had renounced
agnosticism. It is understood that the old man
left some property and a most equitable will, and
that the securities deposited with him for safe-
keeping by the numerous persons who regarded
him as a model of integrity, are all in perfect
order.

When Deeming was in Sydney he occupied
a, house in the vicinity of Petersham. There
was a large stretch of asphalt in front of the house,
and one day, for no visible reason, the incipient
Baron Swanston had the asphalt torn up and the
ground dug over and planted with small fruit
trees. Then one night the servant went out after
dark, and Deeming went out close behind her.
According to current reports, he. had an unaccount-
able aversion to this girl; anyhow, she never came
back and nobody ever saw or heard of her
again. A day or two later, Deeming tore
nip the fruit trees and turned the ground
into a lawn. The grass has flourished there
•ever since which possibly proves that the
ground is well suited for grass ; or, again, it may
be an evidence that dead servant-girl is a good
foundation to construct a lawn upon. Anyhow,
it might be as well to look under these grass-roots
and see if the lost slavey is there. Nobody digs a
lawn as a rule, and regarded as a place to deposit
a deceased friend in it is better than a hearthstone
by a very large percentage. This is The Bulle-
tin’s own special horror, and is probably as big a
lie as most of tiie other horrors, and the daily
papers are hereby warned not to publish portraits
■of that slavey or otln nvise interfere with the
•copyright.

At Her Majesty's, in Sydney, they are
playing “Nowadays,” and it’ takes the public
fancy in various kinds of ways, though why it
bears the name it does no human soul can tell
for it might be designated “Gosh ” and get there
just as well. And it doesn’t matter that, in spots,
the drama’s weak and ill, for the joy is ever kept
alive by Rignold’s brother, Bilk The baronet in
the story is a fiend without remorse, and he gets
in awful trouble through embezzling a horse.
He’s an evil-minded noble with a bitter grin and
scowl, and all the seven devils that vociferously
do howl round Iscariot in Tophet to all eternity
are as mild as seven curates by comparison with
he. But the mills of God grind surely, and in the
present case that mill, is close upon his track
disguised as Rignold’s brother, Bilk And even
when he rises, with language full of ire, and
unjustly bids his son to start at once for endless
fire, and ejects him with a waddy and language
full of hate, and swears when he is struck upon
the stomach with a gate, and tells his friends in
general to go to and grill, still the crowd
gets broken-winded cheering Rignold’s brother,
Bilk The funny man is dreary and washy in the
head, and his jokes are horrible to hear and
mostly made of lead, and his mirth is like
the discords of a wild - eyed German band,
that bangs on brazen cymbals in a bleak
and barren land. And the race - horse, in
this paragraph already mentioned—the animal
by the noble aforesaid—is not a wild
and foaming steed that leaves the wind behind,
but just a solemn crow-bait somewhat vacant in
its mind. And the fair and gentle maiden who
gathers up her trunk—her father turns up later in
a haggard state and drunk—and flies by night in
secret—we have met her oft before, and (we say it
in a whisper) she begins to be a bore; for, long as
we have known her, we have never met her yet
when she didn’t get abducted by the wicked
baronet. But regardless of these evils the play
goes with a will, for a thing of joy and gladness is
Rignold’s brother Bilk The funny man may
perish—likewise the leading dude, and the baronet
may lose himself —he isn’t any good ; also the
bloodshot crowd that bursts a door up in its rage,
and jumps with both its feet upon two welshers
on the stage; and a harmless man who stops a
hole to keep the wind away, and a few assorted
parents who are not altogether gay, and a youth
who wants to die by reason of a big cigar, and
various other people, we can’t remember who
they are—from all of them, if need be, we will
tear ourselves away. And the only thing we ask
is, on the resurrection day, when gathering in the
blackened air with footsteps fast and fleet, the
ghosts that tread on nothing with their silent,
fleshless feet, in myriads are assembled on spectral
wings to rise—then we ask with all due reverence
that somewhere in the skies, amid the bowers of
asphodel or by the jasper sea, where the sun-
shine never fadeth to all eternity, or in that
golden city, which is set upon a hill, we may
come across *he hoofprints of Rignold’s brother
Bilk

“A Sailor’s Knot” will fade away out of
the Royal playbill this week to make room for
“ Master and Man.” Meanwhile in the last days
of the dying drama the British tar is getting up
sundry arrears of loose and disintegrated whoop-
ing over the glorious flag of Britain, which invari-
ably waves wherever it is hoisted, provided there
happens to be a breeze about. The stage-tar, of
the good old times, always does whoop in this
misguided style; as for the real one he would just
as soon have thought of walking head downwards
on the ceiling, and his chief anxiety was to pick
the worms out of the biscuits. Moreover he was
mostly a sad and silent man whose eyes were
strained with looking at that very small object,
his salary, by an exceedingly dim light, and he
filled in the rest of his time by sleeping in a
flooded bunk and being flogged. Therefore the
stage seaman is an outrage, and this paper regards
him with an unfriendly eye.
*m * *
Throughout this week Sydney Criterion
has been filled with great masses of people all
bearing up manfully against a great and heart-
rending grief. It is the last week of Anson—
unless he is re-engaged with great difficulty for
exactly six nights more—and that ancient
favourite is reluctantly tearing himself away as
Eccles, in “Caste.” Our own private opinion is
that when Anson is about to play this character
he sleeps for six nights in the Domain, and joins
in the rush of unemployed at the soup-kitchen in
order to give tone and colouring to the picture.
Possibly, however, it is on account of the actor’s
emotion that Eccles seems, if possible, a little
more bleary this time than usual. Cn previous
occasions lie looked like a gentleman who had
been scooped up in a dredge and educated in a
mud-punt; this time he looks like a gentleman
who had been thrown out into a dog-kennel and
not educated at all. He is the most maudlin and
the most wildly-gifted drunk in all the range of
comedy, a moral, beery clown, full of noble senti-
ments and old boots and stale liquor and disinte-
grated whiskers. When lie is gone there will bo a
woeful gap on the Australian stage, for no such
joyous inebriate ever struck this country before.
Mrs. Brough, Miss Pattie Browne, and Brough
himself, as the gasfitter with the lofty soul, also
bring down the house regularly in the intervals
between Anson’s bursts of intoxication, and the
cast in general is above reproach.

** * »
At Sydney Royal Standard, Monsieur
D’A re’s wonderful artificial performers deserve to


be a bigger draw than they have so far proved.
The mechanism of the fantoccini is perfect and
under marvellous control, the stage appointments
are picturesque and well-managed, and the whole
entertainment crackles with fun and burlesque
horrors. The feats of the magnetic skeletons
which disperse their limbs and skulls variously
and reunite at the sound of mortal presence would
be harrowing, if seen through a blurred atmos-
phere and a mist of whisky strengthened; the bull-
dogs that bite the bobby and strip that officer
to his shirt are gorgeous counterfeits ; and the
bull that tosses the clown into the middle of next
century cannot be too highly commended. There
is a grand Turk who falls to pieces and becomes
one judge and half-a-dozen barristers ; boneless
spectres and astral footmen and impish “komikal
kures,” and an aquarium full of those kind of sea-
monsters that have their birth in a supper-salad.
Altogether, the D’Arc show is the best of its kind
ever seen in Sydney.

The Dan Tracy Vaudeville Company at
Sydney School of Arts fattens on its popularity,
and a new end-man has been introduced in the
person of J. D. Mack, who displays good all-
round quality as comedian, soloist, and foot-
artist. At Sydney Polytechnic, Hiscocks’ Federal
Minstrels have been re-organised with added
delectation to the public; a notable cosmoraraa
lias been sandwiched into the popular nightly
performances ; and Horace Bent, as usual,
exposes the lining of his gizzard to the
public eye. The Alhambra is drawing good
congregations to its concert, olio, and farce,
“ Hard to Kill,” the English comique,
Callaghan, and the “ mockamoor ” Charles Faning
being immense “ takes.” The Bondi and Oogee
Aquariums afford popular satisfaction. The
Cyclorama still draws, having affixed to its pro-
gramme a song, “On Guard,” given with gusto by
Roy Malcolm, in full uniform on the battlefield,
after which he is righteously shot and ambulanced.
“ The Windsor Tragedy ” has been added to the
Waxworks, George-street, the scene representing
“ Williams ” doing the hearthstone-cache trick.
