The performance of “ Masks and Faces,’’
at Melbourne Opera House, goes far to atone for
many insults heaped upon the grey hairs of that
respectable comedy. Local critics, who have
found excellence in cruel misrepresentations of
the same work, blushed (let us hope) as they
decorated their latest notices with the soiled and
faded eulogies of yore. For the first time these
many years, “ Masks and Faces ” is receiving fair
treatment on the Australian stage. Its chief
characters appeal to the heart, and its little crowd
of minor supporters are satisfactory items in the
bill. The story is old-fashioned, and familiarity
has bred contempt for the impossible picture
incident in Triplet’s garret, but the bright
dialogue and subtle character studies of the
Reade-Taylor play will always make it a fine sub-
ject for artists of real ability. Peg Woffington,
in the hands of Mrs. Bernard-Beere, is an
irresistible sixty-eight inches, or more, of winsome
Irish womanhood—warm-hearted, joyous, brilliant,
tender and brave. The change from weary Lena
Despard to lively Peg is striking, and the actress
who died of poison on Friday seems to have been
born again with a new manner, smile, bearing,
almost another voice on Saturday. A very evident
improvement in health enables Mrs. Beere to do
herself fuller justice in the lighter part. After
dancing a jig she showed some signs of exhaustion,
but there is no historical reason for supposing that
the real Woffington didn't get knocked up on such
occasions. Those who were disappointed with the
British Bernhardt in a sickly drama will perceive
her genius at a glance in this wholesome comedy.
Herbert Standing’s conception of Triplet, the
poor old dreamer, is equally natural and
convincing. Here and there his accent hardens,
and a word betrays Standing to the au-
dience, but, save for these occasional lapses,
the pathetic figure of cultured destitution
is Triplet himself. A better get-up for the part
can’t be imagined. The identity of Marius admits
of no change, anyway, and so Sir Charles Poman-
der is a Frenchman (a genial reminder of
Duqnesne) with all the national talent for speak-
ing with his hands and eyebrows. Otho Stuart
is a beautiful but soft kind of Ernest Vane, whilst
Mrs. Urquhart is also a lovely, feeble suggestion
of the neglected wife. Royce, Frank Emery,
Miss Watson and two of the Pelham children are
the most attractive features in a well-finished
background, and the mounting is worthy of the
performance. Mrs. Beere has one noticeable
fault, by the way. She might drop her voice con-
siderably when speaking asides, and still be
audible all over the house.
* *
“ Sunlight and Shadow ” was received
with gentle satisfaction at the Bijou on Saturday.
It is the slim, genteel narrative (already told in
Sydney) of the typical mature lover who thought
his bad wife was dead when she wasn’t, and is
condemned to suffer grief and pain until that in-
convenient interloper passes hence. The bene-
volent doctor of tradition is also there, and the
stereotyped idiot with a farcical passion for the
frolicsome sister is another reminiscence of those
other comedies from which the average actor-
turned-author, like Carton, invariably takes bis
material. As a well-acted, well-balanced trifle,
“ Sunlight and Shadow ” deserves respect. Miss
Ida Liston made a light but pleasing first impres-
sion, and Miss Emma Temple did well indeed as
the maiden who pines in the shadow for awhile.
For the rest, Titheradge, Cecil Ward, Dion Bouci-
cault, Miss Watt-Tanner, and Arthur Lawrence
were nice as usual, although it is about time that
the latter member of the company did something
fresh with his hands. He has rubbed the novelty
off them.
* * * *
“Bridget O’Brien” reappeared at Mel-
bourne Royal, on Saturday, when there was a
considerable rush to meet the grand old daughter
of Erin and give her a warm reception. There is
no necessity to paint the rampageous lily or gild
her heart of unrefined gold. Bridget has got past
the need of such vanities. Her reputation is
founded upon a rock, and her humour is as ever-
lasting as the wurrm which never dieth. It seems
a pity that a woman of her calibre can’t go through
bfe unattended by a variety show, because such
things arc uncertain and subject to fits of melan-
choly. The fag-end of Saturday’s entertainment,
unrelieved by any glimpses of the immortal
Widow and Mother-in-law, was insufferably dull.
After 12 depressing females, called French Guards,
nad been walking around the stage for about 15
minutes the public began to clear out. However,
a change of variety show is easy to manage, and if
J. F. Sheridan takes care to leave out a number of
attractions and'politely requests several people to
desist, the present season ought to prove fairly
productive.
** * *
At the Princess’s they have enjoyed good
houses for “Dorothy,” although the temporary
retirement of Ryley (replaced by Sydnej Deane)
doesn’t make matters go any faster. On Saturday
next (9th.) the actual last hours of Elton will bo
inaugurated by a revival of “The Gondoliers.”
On Wednesday (13th.) they revert to “The Old
Guard,” and on Thursday, the well-beloved little
comedian will benefit in the character of Folydore
Poupart and bid adieu to Australia. Nobody
knows why Elton should leave like this on the eve
of a general election. He might take his choice
of boroughs and waltz to the top of the poll if the
spirit so moved him. And once in Parliament the
only William would be expected to run the entire
Cabinet in his own person, and permitted to in-
crease his salaries all round up to any reasonable
total.
w« « «
Dampier is another idol of the masses who
could get in for anywhere by a large majority, but
he prefers to devote his talents to booming
“ Wilful Murder.” The crime on the hearthstone
does a steady business, and promises to last until
Easter. One night, last week, Miss Rounsevell
called at the theatre and saw Dampier, and said
that the drama made her shudder when she
thought of all that might have been. There is
some talk of petitioning the Chief Secretary to
give “Williams ” a night off in order that he, too,
may see “Wilful Murder” and enjoy himself
again, but the treat is not likely to come off.
Meanwhile, the Alexandra Williams misses a
point by not pulling his sham moustache out by
the roots, but he is right up to date with “ Gogd
bye, ladies !” when his gory career is arrested by
the law. Other shows are girding up their loins
for the holidays.

He played Othello when Miln took over the part of lago

It used to be industriously reported, also quite com-
monly believed, that Ryley had brought a devoted wife
and family to Australia. Fancy loved to picture him in
the act of romping with five merry children on the hearth-
rug, whilst the smiling mother darned their socks, and
cut little jokes about those ridiculous sassiety women who
were vainly hoping to undermine poor Charley’s domestic
virtues. For the sake of this refreshing fiction, one feels
sorry to hear that the “ favourite baritone ” came to Aus-
tralia without any encumbrances except his luggage, and
has never romped on the hearthrug in the manner stated.
The " Human Fly” is a lady to appear in Sydney and
Melbourne shortly. She walks head-downwards, with
air-exhauster goloshes.
Harwood, in Melbourne, keeps clean-shaved, whereas
he always grows a long black beard when he has deter-
mined to relinquish the smell of the floats; but people say
the beard would be as snow-white now a-days as G. B. W.
Lewis’s.
Spagnoletti: “I’m hanged if I can enjoy the acting of a
lot of demireps, who seem to be only going over their own
lives in a spectral way.”
Coppin came first to Melbourne from Launceston with
a company including Mr. and Mrs. G. 11. Rogers and Mr.
and Mrs. Charles Young. Both Rogers and Young were
afterwards rather better known in Sydney than in Mel-
bourne. Mr. and Mrs. Young went to England, where
they divorced, and the lady married Yezin, with whom
she got on little better. Forty-eight years ago Young
made a good Claude Melnotte, and he was greatly fancied
as William, in “ Black-eyed Susan,” but later he settled
into a low comedian. Mrs. Young married him at 15
.years old, greatly against the wish of her mother, Mrs.
Thomson; and her sister was our old-time Sydney
favourite, Mrs. C. Jones. Coppin introduced the first
carpet on the Melbourne stage, at the Queen’s Theatre,
the play being the “ School for Scandal.”
Meston expects to have his 25 Queensland niggers ready
to start for Chicago in July or August.
Mrs. Nye-Chart (Miss Nellie Rollasson) of the Theatre
Royal, Brighton (Eng.), is dead. It will be remembered
that it was to her that Lefroy, the murderer, last offered
his play. When she refused it, he determined to rob and
murder.
Gilbert Hare, the promising son of John Hare, is
coming to Australia for his health.

that “ ladies have fainted and strong men have been
carried away.”
W. H. Jude’s first Sunday lecture at Hobart being a
pronounced success the talented composer held three
more, and intends resuming them shortly. Despite his
violent denunciations of sectarian Christianity and his
sneers at orthodoxy, he has been received on each
occasion with literally overflowing houses.

The most delightful thing in theatrical experience is to
elevate a secondary character into the first place, Such
was the treat of Gwendolen Floyd v. Laura Villiers in the
“Dancing Girl.” How Brooke did squelch M’Kean
Buchanan, in Melbourne, by acting lago to his Othellol
Befnard-Beere’s death-scene in “As in a Looking
Glass ” is apparently borrowed from Mdlle. Croizette’s in
“The Sphinx,” with the same trick of turning the face a
greenish hue. Croizette and Bernhardt acted in the piece
together, but the former has abandoned the stage tor
some years.

One handles Watts-Phillips’ newly-published biography
with a kindly remembrance of his daughter, Roland Watts-
Phillips. After long and weary waiting, with his play of
“Joseph Ohavigny ” on hand, Phillips gav.e it to a copyist,
named Hastings, for re-production, and Hastings caused
Webster to buy it, as also two other plays, “The Poor
Strollers” and “ Dead* Heart.” Yet was Phillips nearly
worried to death over the delays in production, and his
prodigious success of “Lost in London” might not have
come out hut for being pirated in America. Roland was
named after Madame Roland of the French Revolution,
Phillips being a French enthusiast.

When Macready was young, he complained to his
father, the Birmingham manager, about his neglect of
one ot the actors who shone brilliantly in a casual per-
formance by a small travelling Co. “ Ah,” says the old
man, “my company reduces him to his level”—and so
it was.
G. V. Brooke’s English creditors did all they could to
save his life, for two sheriff’s officers pursued him on
board the London, and Brooke only escaped through
Capt. Martin disguising him as a sailor. Running round
with the others, at the capstan, he would chuckle over
his lucky escape, as he made up once more for William, in
“ Black-Eyed Susan.” Did he think of this, as he stood,
in his red shirt, by the pumps, in the Bay of Biscay?
The long derelict Lyceum Theatre, in Pitt-street,
Sydney, is under way again, the ground lease of the late
owner having been made over to Mr. Walter Ives by the
Equity Court last week. The new building, which is
designed on the lines of the Melbourne Princess’s, will be
ready for occupancy in about six months’ time, and will, they
say, possibly open under the management of a gentleman
whose front names are James Cassius. We have not heard
the surname.
The small boys of Melbourne are off maib’cs in (lie
meantime, also they have discarded cricket and turned
their hearts from football. The all-prevailing pastime of
childhood is a new game called “ Windsor Murder.” In
every school-ground, and in many a grimy backyard, the
slaughter of Mrs. Williams takes place twice a day, or
oftener. Generally speaking, the Windsor tragedy is a
simple, primitive entertainment, but here and there a
high-class performance is got up by a boy of intellect. In
the first place, an urchin (“boss’’-eyed if obtainable),
disguised in a burnt-cork moustache and a hideous scowl,
ignites a heap of dry twigs so as to mark the position of
the fire-place. The lid of a packing case does duty for
the hearthstone, and after much stealthy glancing over
his shoulder, the intended assassin shifts this lid and digs
a shallow grave. His work completed the infant Williams
observes, “ That’ll do.” Hammer in one hand, and a
blunt pocket-knife in the other, the miscreant slinks off
in quest of his prey. Reclining against a tree he finds a
lay figure, composed of a sack tied round a piece of
clothes-prop and surmounted by an old straw h it.
Williams dissembles a good deal at the outset. With
ghastly tenderness he remarks “ It’s a fine day, dear,” to
the unsuspecting scarecrow ; likewise he laughs gaily and
takes a drink from a medicine-bottle. The bottle (which
he has just filled at the school tap) contains neat brandy
calculated to brace him up for the bloody deed. Then he
fells Mrs. Williams with the hammer, administers a horrid
gash under the straw hat, and drags the murdered sack
away to the fireplace. Williams, like all actors who have
got hold of a rich hit of “fat,” would fain linger over the
interment, but voices in the distance are, by this time,
exhorting him to hurry up. So he stamps the corpse
down into the cavity, readjusts the hearthstone, and seals
the awful transaction with cement. After which he
decamps to the West Australian extremity of the
yard, and engages in mining pursuits at a salary
of six apples per week. Presently a mob of
bo3S stroll casually up to the hearthstone, and
pinch their nostrils. They smell something. The aroma
proceeds from the hearthstone, which is raised with great
apocryphal difficulty, and the corpse of Mrs. W. comes
to light. “ Who has done this orful murder?” says the
boy cast for the character of first citizen. They all rush
off to fetch a detective and inform the ’Ekald. The
detective is usually the star performer and stage-
manager. He wears a lawn-tennis hat pulled over his
eyes, and the longest overcoat he can borrow. He is the
embodiment of gloomy mystery and dark suspicion.
After a careful examination ot the remains, the tec. cuts
off a fragment ot sack (observing that it is a clue) and
retires into the woodshed to make enquiries. Thereupon,
the horror-struck citizens dig another hole, a proper
cemetery grave, nine inches deep, and bury Mrs. Williams
amidst vigorous manifestations of woe. ’ The detective
now emerges from the woodshed, carrying a lantern
and a pocket - book, and proceeds to hunt the
criminal down. He has obtained a photograph
of liis father, from the family album, which
will enable him to identify Williams on the spot, conse-
quently a number of boys stand in a row to he compared
with Ihe portrait. “ The man I’m after ain’t here,” says
the officer, in tones that seem to issue from the bottom of
his overcoat. For the moment he is baffled like. Then
he goes on the prowl in a zig-zag line followed by the
general public until at last he turns his lantern on Wil-
liams, and recognises his man by the photo. “In the
name of Lord Hopetoun yer my prisner! ” groans the
detective. Williams is further identified by splashes of
mud-pie on the back of his hands, and a morsel of the
original sack inside his hoot. Anyway he has got to be
hanged. The detective fastens a clothes-line under his
arms, throws the ends over a horizontal bar, or a fence,
and Williams is hauled up and dropped again with a
sickening thud ! But just previous to execution the
villain is expected to confess all. “ How many other
people have you murdered?” asks the detective. “A
hundred and eleven,” says Williams. “ Launch his soul
into Eternity,” says the detective.

Frank Boyle, a tenor who made his Australian dehut
coincidently with Madame Melba, has died at Barnstaple,
Eng., aged 33 years. Melbournites will remember him as
the tenor of the Royal Comic Opera Company.
The Dan Barry Dramatic Co. has done good biz. at
Hillgrove, Armidale, and Tenterfield, N.S.W., and, at
latest advices, had crossed the Queensland border to
Stanthorpe. The plays relied on have been “ Ransom,”
“ The Parson’s Oath,” and “ Madame Midas.” Dan
yanks his bull-dog, “Paddy,” into every play; in
“ Ransom ” he palms him off as “a poodle.”
Charity in England nowadays is a virtue
that needs to be dragged up by the hair to make
it grow at all. Few people have any idea of the
frantic struggles that are required to keep the
Salvation and similar funds from utter extinction.
Early this year the London G.P.O. was almost
blocked by 100,000 begging letters which the
“Army” had posted in one fatal heap. During
last year Dr. Barnardo sent out a trifle over a
million printed and written appeals for money ;
and other charities or alleged charities carry on
the great cadging industry with almost equal
vigour. Most of the money received by a large
number of these institutions goes in circulars
asking for more money and in postage stamps,
and the employment they provide is mostly to
broken-down clerks who are hired at competitive
rates to address begging letters at so much the
thousand.
Frank Hayes, of Auckland (M.L ), com-
mitted suicide a fortnight ago. First of all he
loaded his gun and tied a ponderous weight upon
the trigger. Another string was attached to the
weight and tied securely to a hook overhead.
This second string was wrapped round the centre
of a candle, and when the candle burned half
through the string would naturally combust, and
then the weight would descend with a portentous
thud, and the gun would go off, and Frank being
at the end of it, would go off likewise. These
arrangements being completed, Mr. Hayes
lighted the candle, arranged the gun with the
muzzle resting on his pillow, lay down with his
head directly in front of it, and took chloroform.
By-and-bye the gun went off, and there the busi-
ness, so far as concerned Hayes himself, was
finished. About a week later Ins landlord burst
the door in while searching for his rent, and took
an inventory of the remains. There was one bed,
one table, one corpse with most of the head gone,
a rusted gun with the muzzle tipped up in the air,
and a weight hanging from the trigger, half a
candle, an empty bottle, and some hair embedded
in the wall.
Jimmy Tyson’s before-lunch transaction
with Treasurer MTiwraith (Q.), when he cooky
purchased <£200,000 worth of Treasury-bills, lias
started a batch of yarns concerning the bachelor-
millionaire. Once, up North, Tyson wanted to
transmit £50,000 to a bank in another province.
The local manager would not at first make any
reduction .in the usual rate of exchange. Tyson
apparently caved in, but a minute later wrote out
a cheque—“Pay self, £300,000.” “ I want it in
gold,” said he, “and at once, please.” The
manager became the suppliant; the other matter
was at once reasonably arranged, and the £300,000
cheque was torn up. A Sydney firm of auctioneers
once offered Tyson £lOOO if he would attend a
land-sale and make a single bid, the idea being
that his apparent anxiety to buy would ensure a
big all-round success. He refused. At Too-
woomba (Q.) railway-station, a broken-down old
mate of Tyson once saw the latter counting over a
big roll of notes. “James,” said he, “one of
those would do me a lot of good just now, and
you’d never miss it.” Turning his back, and
holding out the handful of notes behind him,
Tyson said, laconically, “Help youfself.” And
so forth, in wonderful variation.
The Wild-Cat Column.
A mining bank established on a big basis would
be a sound investment in N.S.W., where the ordinary
banking institutions, under all circumstances, evince tha
strongest aversion to mining securities. Controlled by
mining men and conducted by managers of mining
experience, it would be as safe as any other form o!
banking, it was the means of the success of the Bank ot
Victoria.

A sign of the times. Till recently the janitor
ofN.S. W. Stock Exchange was an individual in all the
glory of uniform and brass buttons. He hath departed.
Even members envied his billet. If the secretary resigned
his £3OO a year, somebody would be killed in the rush of
members for the berth.
* * *
The public are hearing a little too much of the
wonders of the Emerald Proprietary mine, and much too
little of the real value of the emeralds already extracted.
* * *
Mount Poole is to some shareholders a
“ Marvel.” To us it is the same old story. A rich,
narrow surface-patch. Sensationally rich crushing of
carefully-selected stone, on which is built up a mountain
of exaggeration to which interested local and innocently-
gulled metropolitan newspapers largely contribute.
Company formed, and legitimate work only performed
when the gold has left. The rest—a rapid, inglorious
death, and feeble attempts at resurrection.
* * #
The Manar (N.S.W.) Sluicing Co.’s ground and
race—the latter cost upwards of £l2,ooo—have been sold
for £4OO. The purchasers have joined hands with the
purchasers of the Yellow Springs, an alluvial property on
the Shoalhaven which has long languished for the want
of water gravitation. It is proposed to construct a dam
at the head, enlarge the race (which is 22 miles long),
continue it another 20 miles to Yellow Springs and work
the two properties conjointly. A big American mining
man has, after careful examination, declared the project
a genuine one, and there is little doubt some Melbourne
men will put up the money.
* * *
Public opinion often gets abend of the best
mining men. Notwithstanding the vaunted declarations
of safety and permanence of the Broken Hill Proprietary
Mine, public opinion judges it insecure and sees the
dividend end at the sulphide levels. Shares have fallen
and continue to fall.
* * *
We are asked for an opinion of the Sir Walter
Scott Mine, Cancri, floated two years ago. The reef is
poor in gold, which is of low value. There are other
mines in the country of which much is made of the number
of ozs. while the value of the gold is carefully concealed.
» # •
Another evolution in White Hock—the mine
and plant knocked down at £3OOO. Ten times the amount
would not cover the cost of the plant. On anolher occa-
sion upwards of that amount was knocked down on a trip
to the mine. It was a magnificent picnic- special trains,
coaches, free hotel accommodation, enough champagne
to bathe in. Remembrances of that trip cause us to shed
a tear—for those who paid for it.
* * #
Will it make or ruin the purchasers is a query
which time and more money alone will answer. Howell
said it would pay if his recommendations concerning
plant were followed. Upwards of £20,000 was spent in
that direction without success. The writer said of it in
its very infancy that the ore was of too low a grade ever
to pay interest on a million. Lots of people who didn’t!
believe this were ruined. One man alone who made up-
wards of £20,000 on the Barrier lost all of it in White
Rock. It ruined whole families in Adelaide, and by many
others is only remembered as a curse. If one-fiftieth
part of the money wasted in machinery had been spent in
experimental treatment elsewhere the plant would never
have been erected.
# # *
One of the owners of the Last Call mine pinned
a quantity of its scrip—which, by the way, was like that
of many other wild cats, a gorgeous document—to the
wall of the Empire Hotel, and turning to Mark Hammond
exclaimed; “It only wants some Taranganbas beside iti
to complete the picture.” “It is not a Taranganba.
without your misrepresentation and deception,” replied
Hammond. “Look here, Hammond,” said the other
fellow, “ whatever becomes of it recollect you bought ther
mine with your eyes open. You were allowed to prospecti
where you liked and as you liked.” Hammond was
deceived not so much by what he did as by what he failed
to do.
* * *
Every year incubates some new modes of crush-
ing, yet the old stamper-battery used by the ancients
reigns supreme. It is admittedly an engineering mistake
simply because power is required to lift the stampers and
crushing-power is only obtained by the dropping of then-
own weight, yet nothing has been found to take their
place.
* * *
America has nothing to teach Australia in
stamp-milis. After travelling through the States, a
Victorian mining engineer not only failed to find out any
improvement in their mills, but brought back orders for
stamp-batteries made on the Victorian patterns. After
many years this design is re-introduced as the great Cali-
fornian stamp-mill.
# * m
What was described as the latest thing in stamp-
mills was imported from Chicago for the White Rock
mine. Upon arrival it was found to be of a pattern long
discarded in Australia, where an infinitely superior plant
could have been obtained for less than half the price.
* * *
Many people would like to know if there is no
danger of the thousands of tons of soft wood in the Broken
Hill mines catching fire, and if there is any means of put-
ting it out should a fire break out, A lire in a mine is
the most disastrous of all fires, inasmuch as the smoke
prevents it being got at; hence it is that some mines
have burnt themselves out, and have taken a mighty long
time about it.
* * *
Is it true that the filling-up system has been
recently vigorously started at Broken Hill, and that in
some dangerous places, for the sake of expedition, what
are known as pig-styes have been built and filled in with
good ore ?
# * #
In the boom, if not now, several of the big
brokers of Adelaide, Melbourne and Sydney had workmen
in the Broken Hill mines who were paid to watch and
wire things. In the golden mines of Bendigo. Ballarat,
or Charters Towers, a mine manager would find it a hard
thing to get ahead of his miners.
# * *
The N.S.W. Stock Exchange seems to be on its
last logs, and, to all appearances, some of its members are
wearing their last double-decker.
* © *
The holder of several thousands of pounds worth
of shares in a N.S.W. mine, quoted as saleable at, let us
say, £1 a share, grumbled to the writer, the oilier day,
that he was hard-up. On being asked why he didn’t sell
some of his scrip and put himself in funds, he replied,
“Ah ! you don't know the game. I have the reputation
of being a shrewd man, and so long as I hold, the public
hack me and keep the shares at their price. If 1 sold ICO,
they’d be back to five shillings. When we are crushing
with good returns then the public will back the mine;
not until then.”
# * #
Silver has now touched 3s. 3d. per oz.. a fall of
4i[d. per oz. since January 1 of this year. Taking the
weekly yield of the Broken Hill Proprietary mine at
200,0000 z., this represents a diminution in value of £3958
per’ week, or £17,000 per calendar month—£2o4,ooo per
annum. The Proprietary has hitherto paid £96,000
monthly in dividends. The weekly yield lias been in-
creased’by 20,0000 z, since the beginning of the year, and
the average of silver to the ton of ore has also increased.

A Broken-Hill man made £BO,OOO out of
a boom in certain shares, in consequence of infor-

Mr. C. G. Heydon takes an acting N.S.W.
Supreme Court Judgeship. He is a middle-aged
man of many years practice, and is recognised as
a safe, careful, and hideously conscientious mem-
ber of the Sydney bar.
It is affecting to hear that bachelor Way,
S.A. Chief-Justice, has a pet-dog which accom-
panies him everywhere. It sits on a sofa in his
chambers in the day-time, and nestles up against
the feet end of Samuel’s couch at night.
Millionaire Shaw, who died in London a
few weeks ago, was the most renowned proprietor
of “ bucket-shops.” He made £200,000 a year by
share-gambling houses. People speculated with
him in future time purchases—cash down.
New Plymouth (M.L.) elected the borough
lamp-lighter as its member in Parliament. This
luminary—which his name is Smith—only weighs
about eight stone, but he carried enough brawn to
knock out a burly butcher who offended him.
In Adelaide, when remarks unflattering
to W. G. Grace were being made, one of the team,
Radcliffe, is alleged to have said, “ Why do you
growl at the Doctor? He has only been out here
twice ; we have to put up with his manners every
year! ”
Edward Ward, litterateur and broker,
dropped dead, in Adelaide, last week. Doctors
said he died from heart disease, but it was
“broken” heart. Ward was at one time a
prominent member of Liverpool (Eng.) Reform
League, and was an uncompromising Freetrader.
E. B. L Dickens, son of Charles Dickens,
is member for Wilcannia in N.S.W. Assembly.
The other night Willis, M.P, for Bonrke, who is
addicted to blatherskiting, made a Jong speech.
Dickens followed him and remarked : “ My late
father is reported to have said, 'Barkis iswiffin’,’
but if he were here now he would say, ‘ Wilds is
barkin’.’” ' «
Jack Woods, of Victorian politics, is gone,
aged 70. He never could outlive the tar-brush
incident, an apparently groundless accusation of
tarring water-pipes to fill up cracks when an em-
ploye of the Victorian Water Supply Department,
He professed to be converted, by a visit to India
with Bent and Bosisto, from his views in favour of
opening the Public Library on Sundays, but he
was a cunning old dog. He got married again
only last year.
Alfred Peach Hensman, who has accepted
the position of Third Judge of the Supreme Court
of Western Australia, came to the province in 1883’,
as Attorney-General. He resigned office in 1885,
being unable to agree with Governor Broome and
bis “one-man Government” of the province.
Since that period Hensman has been practically
the leader of the Bar at Perth, and has fearlessly
advocated democratic principles. He is an
enthusiastic musician, and is honorary conductor
of the Perth Musical Union.

An Irish peer died the other day of in-
fluenza. We forget his name at this present
moment, but it doesn’t matter. The only thing
ho was celebrated for was that, when lie was
married, Punch found a rhyme for the name of his
wife’s father. The father had a very awkward
name, and no one had ever found a rhyme for it
before. We forget what the old man was called
now, but it is of no consequence either. It was a
poor thing to be celebrated for, anyhow, but it
was the only point about him that anybody re-
membered, so all the English papers are careful to
mention it in his obituary. Probably if it had

Dr. Wilhelm Junker, the African explorer,
who died the other day, left behind him this
memorable statement :
It is not necessary for an African explorer to kill people
right and left, like a conqueror in an enemy’s land. I
never killed anyone, and mostly travelled without
a weapon, and still achieved what I wanted.
Perhaps it was_ because he never killed anybody
that Junker died almost unknown. And just
here a picture rises before us of a man with a
heavy jowl and dyed hair, and a Bible in his
strong right hand, wading through a sea of gore
and bowels and flogged niggers, to a niche in the
temple of everlasting fame, with the much-
travelled Smythe for his fugleman, and Wales
waiting to receive him in evening-dress on his
return.
There was not long ago, in Melbourne, a
young Hebrew clergyman named Isidor Myers, a
man who lived for others, and who was Rabbi of
the most orthodox 'Jewish congregation in Vic-
toria. He was never tired of declaiming against
usury and the ignominy which it inflicted upon the
race whom God hath certainly chosen, so far as
brains are concerned. “ The Jews,” he said one
day, preaching in English to the Germans,
Russians and Poles who chiefly composed his con-
gregation, “are fit for nobler things. They are
too strong to remain so selfish. Let them cease
worshipping money and look to other and greater
ideals. Let them look, for instance, to Jesus of
Nazareth, who was, after all, a Jew.” It is
extremely probable that the bulk of the Rev.
Isidor Myers’ congregation didn't quite under-
stand him.

Deaceful seclusion at Bendivo in 187 J •-
There are a great many animals (numan) in tins world,
resembling anything almost, rather than the thing they
actually are. But this creature had been hunted by out-
raged humanity from post to pillar, and from a nameless
place to Hackney ; and so he was haggard and restless,
always on the move; nervous, startling, wan, deeply
furrowed, cadaverous and in clothing tattered and torn
by the chase. Being inside the hut awhile I could at
length distinguish what was therein besides the hunted
and haunted man. A couple of giu cases, a concern like
a ship’s tank, on which were some rags and newspapers
(this was the bed), a few kerosene tins, a small wooden
box, some rusty dishes used for mining purposes by
“ hatters ” on the goldfields —these constituted the furni-
ture of the haunted and hunted man’s hut. But a great
branch of gum tree, the top end of which was alight in
the fireplace, the other end reaching almost down to the
other end of the hut, served conveniently to open a con-
versation and to elucidate matters. I asked Fullivan how
it was lie didn’t chop the stick up, instead of burning it
in that queer way. He said he had no axe ; furthermore,
no man in that neighbourhood would lend him an axe ;
also, no man in any neighbourhood would lend him an
axe. And so, little by little I ascertained from Sullban,
in Ills furtive, wild, frightened, nervous way, that lie was
absolutely boycotted by the human race. No one would
hold converse with him, or sell him anything, or lend him
anything. If he did get an odd pennyweight of gold, he
could only dispose of it at one-half of its value. No one
would hear him or answer his “ good day no one wou’d
do anything but stare at him. By degrees Sullivan got
more confidential and reassured, and I heard much from
him that interested me. On the subject of the murders
lie did not long dwell, nor did I press him on these
tragedies, feeling, anyhow, that information from him on
such occurrences would be worse than worthless. Gener-
ally he denied any active participation in any of them.
But of his own mental tortures and sufferings he seemed
not to weary of talking. It appeared he had not very
long returned from the Old Country. On landing at
Liverpool an immense crowd gathered about him, and
audible murmurs of “That’s Sullivan, the murderer!”
buzzed about him wherever lie went. Out of lodging-
houses and public-houses, from wharves, streets, thorough-
fares he was chased by the same awful mutterings—that’s
Sullivan the murderer ! It was to escape this very thing
that he had gone home, but escape from it there was
none. He went into the country. There, for a while, he
had some sort of respite; but very soon the ploughman
and the cowboy began to gaze at him over fences, and he
heard them mutter “ that’s Sullivan the murderer !” At
farm-houses the maidservants ran away from him, crying,
“Oh, God! Sullivan, the murderer!” And children,
going to school, ran hack in terror, to tell their parents
that they had met Sullivan the murderer ! Along with
this, the police dogged his footsteps from one country to
another, from one place to another, from one port to
another, from one town to another. What could he do to
got away from such a horrible hell as this ? What indeed !
There was no get away from it! Like all hunted animals,
he directed his steps towards where lie thought some
semblance of a home lay. He came back to Bendigo.
Several thousand people were at the railway station there,
when he came up from Melbourne, and “that’s Sullivan
the murderer !” was heard as before. At Inglewood the
people got tired of repeating it, and treated him hence-
forth as a dog. What he wanted now was to get away
somewhere without the police knowing whither lie had
gone. There might be a chance, then, of the cry “ that’s
Sullivan the murderer I” no more sounding in his ears.
But there was not the slightest prospect of that, unless
the Government gave absolute directions that he was to
be allowed to go, and that the police were not to know,
or to concern themselves as to his destination. No one
would have truck or trade with this murderer: no one
would sell him anything, much less give him am thine
How then did he live? For the bread-fruit grows not ol
Die gum-tree, and a used-up diggings is a parched mrl
dreary and hungry place withal ! Yes, to he sure von
could not miss thinking it, if you think at all, it was e
woman who supplied Sullivan with food. She came a
long wav every night, in the daikncss, so that no one
should see her. She came with her little bundle of 1,10
visions, and she put them on the threshold of iv
murderer’s door. She tapped tim'dly at the door to let
Sullivan know that his dinner was there, and then she
flitted away into the darkness. Nor storm, nor rain nor
aught else stopped or stayed her ; this timid, dariim’ not
well off, poor little woman. An Irishwoman she" was
and I only mention that fact in order to say that probebl J
she had herself known, and f it, what it was to be elect',
on the high road of this world.
A few years ago, in the glittering days
when borrowed millions were still plentiful in
Maoriland, the constituents of a goldfields mem-
ber resolved to present him with something or
other as a mark of gratitude and esteem. He was
a very silent member in these days, and either he
said nothing at all or else he said something that
wasn’t worth listening to, but he wasageniusatgeb
tinghold of the loan-money, and it was resolved that
he deserved encouragement. Also he was always
hard up, so they took a practical view of things
and sent along a draft for .£lOO. Unluckily they
forgot that, under the bribery and corruption law s
of Maoriland, he was likely to lose his seat if ho
accepted the money. The politician remembered
this fact distinctly, and tore his hair with fury
at the thought of having to return it. Tho'a
a glorious idea struck him. Next, lie cashed the
draft. Finally he wrote a mournful letter in which
he expressed his regret that they should imagine
lie could accept money for his services, and in a
grief-stricken tone he sent the £lOO back per
medium of his own cheque. The constituents
were much impressed by the letter, the local paper
founded an editorial upon it, and it was also
“communicated ” to the press all over the country.
But the cheque, of course, came back from the
bank marked N.S.F., and as the constituents
couldn’t well sue upon it, it remains N.S.F. to
this day.
More than one man, writes “Marooga-
line,” has been chaffed for references to the tree-
climbing fishes of North Queensland, and yet in
some places they are common as frogs, and i have
shot scores on the mangroves at the mouths of the
Russell, Midgrave and Barron Rivers. Ido not
know their southern limit, but Capt. Cook saw
them, or a kindred species, in 177 U, at Thirsty
Sound, opposite the Northumberland Islands.
That is the first mention of this peculiar fish.
They grow up to a foot in length, with a tail
something like an eel, or exactly like that of the
(leratodus Forsteri. By the way, I may mention
here that this remarkable fish, usually known as
the “Burnett salmon,” is named after the late
Win. Forster, of Sydney, who was once squatting
on the Burnett and sent the first specimen to
Gerard Krelft, who named it in 1872. The tree-
climbing fish is not remarkable for beauty. His
eyes are prominent, situated on the top of his
head. When you are passing up a saltwater creek
at low tide they will run across the sand, climb up
a mangrove and ga/.e at you for a minute before
jumping down and rushing into the water again.
The pectoral fins, by which they climb, are really
rudimentary hands, and the fish has either re-
mained stationary for ages, like the Ceratodus, or
is in course of evolutionary transition through
the amphibian stage. In any case he is a
curiosity.
A Funny Game.
A few friends met the other night in a Newtown
store to spend a jovial evening, but as they were
all teetotallers and none of them drank anything
stronger than Clements Tonic, drunkenness was
out of the question. Mac. proposed a game
at euchre, and Jones said they had better play for
something just to give the game an interest and
make it lively, but times were hard, so they did
not care to play for money. Smith, however,
spied a box of Fletcher's bilious and liver pills,
and proposed that each time a man was beaten he
should take a pill, and this made the game lively
and the fun was fast and furious. Simpson
swallowed five, Jones got away with four and
Smith and Hopkins two each and Mac. got off
scot free. The game terminated by a general
exodus at 3 a.m., for Dr. Fletcher’s pills never
labour in vain. They are the best and mildest
pills made and are sold by all chemists and
druggists everywhere; being a positive, safe,
and reliable cure for constipation, liver complaint,
headache, &c. They always give satisfaction.
The proprietors give away £5O (in seven prizes) at
Xmas, for the seven first nearest guesses sent in,
as to how many of these pills a large Clements
Tonic bottle (now full) holds. A guess form is
around every box, and all such forms are eligible
for this competition that come from around a box.
One box of Fletcher’s Pills proves their virtues
and they can be obtained everywhere at Is., or
post free 13 penny stamps, from F. M. Clements,
Newtown, N.S.W.
It is most convenient to have a first-class
room near tram stopping places, where ladies
can rest themselves, and if they choose, have, a
good cup of tea, or other refreshments. We
understand the Country Milk Co.’s “Cascade,”
80 and 82 Elizabeth-street, near King-street, is
just the place ; it is certainly the largest and best-
conducted room in Sydney, and is getting quite
the fashionable resort for afternoon tea. We also
understand that ladies who so desire can have
parcels sent there, so that they can call for them
just before taking the tram for home.*

POLITICAL POINTS
The Victorian general elections will be
neld on 20th instant—next Wednesday week.
Down South, Shiels is spoken of as “ the
maiden Premier’’—presumably because lie has
never been in a coalition.
A verbatim report of Premier Shiels’ cele-
brated Casterton (Vie.) oration would have filled
pi columns of Melbourne Argus.
Sydney Daily Telegraph ascribes the
Australian commercial depression to “blast and
blither.” How about boom and burst?
The opinion (without a fee) of the legal
profession is, that the suspension of the Victorian
Kail way Commissioners was a political outrage.
Said that S A. Premier Playford intends
to resign before Parliament re-opens. Politically
drad politicians: Parkes, Munro, Gillies and
Playford !
John Hurley, lately a N.S.'W. M.P., is
feeling his way with the electors of the Burrinn,
Q., a constituency which he represented some 10
years ago.
Sir Graham Berry is official liquidator of
the Melbourne Mercantile Bank. That is not
quite what ho wants. lie wants to be official
liquidator of Victoria.
The number of persons, and the astound-
ing ignorance of most of them, who were placed
on the list of Js’.P. at last Queensland general
election is past all belief.
“Empire Tea” Harper, when asked at
his meeting whether he was in favour of One-man-
one-vote, said, “Yes, he was in favour of One-
man-one-vote in each electorate .”
Cable : “ The native people of India are
organising with a view of demanding the franchise
and self-government.” Another sign of the con-
tinuous crumbling of the British Empire.
Premier Shiels’ loquacity, his sludge flume,
is intolerable : contrast him with Verdon, who
used to rattle otf a perfect Budget speech in 40
minutes. Nevertheless, with all faults, it is felt
that Shiels is the man of the hour.
Kanaka Griffith’s open confession :
Because certain men will not work, what exclusive
rights have we, any more than Indians. Kanakas, or
Chinamen ?
Speak for yourself, Samuel.
N.S.W, Government has abandoned its
proposed short special session. The main work
promised for next session is—(l) One-man-one-
vote Bill, with Electoral Redistribution measure ;
(2) Local Government Bill; (3) Australian
Federation Bill.
The W.A. Government closed the recent
session of Parliament with a substantial majority
at its back. The relative position of parties was :
—Ministerialists, 21 ; Oppositionists, 9, Reform
of the Constitution is to be. tackled next session,
which will commence in August of this year.
The settlement by the Queensland Govern-
ment of , the Robb railway-contract lawsuit, in
which plaintiff claimed over £104,000, and agreed
to take =£loo,ooo in 41 per cent. Treasury Bills,
has the appearance of being an expedient to get
rid of an awkward difficulty at an equally
awkward time.
Premier Griffith (Q.) has squared the
direct Opposition, which chiefly consisted of the
Squatting Party, by his conversion to Kanaka
labour, and has only a few independent bppoennts
from amongst his former alleged Liberal follow-
ing. Judas himself flourished for a short period,
but Nemesis came in due course.
The Sydney Exhibition Building was
erected under the old Foreign-trade regime to dis-
play the arts .and manufactures of N.S. Wales to
an astonished world. It is now used as a place
for the shoeless dead-beat to sleep in. He is the
principal manufacture of N.S. Wales, so the
structure is fulfilling its purpose to the letter.
Billy Brookes, M.L.C. (Q.), prophesies
that:—
In ten years’ time Kanakas will not be available, and
the position of Queensland will then be ten times worse
than it is now ; while there will also be a vested interest
in coloured labour so great, that coolies or Chinamen will
be substituted for Polynesians.
J. C. Neild, Garrard, and other Ms.P.
were quite in the right in opposing a special par-
liamentary grant to St. Vincent’s Hospital,
Sydney, which, although an admirable institution,
is, from a purely political standpoint, a private
establishment of a sectarian character. It is not
subject to Government supervision, and, on that
ground, the Committee of Supply was illogical
and inconsistent in passing the vote. If St, Vin-
cent’s wants State aid it ought to bring itself
within the provisions of the Hospitals Act without
delay. Similar arguments would apply to Church
af England, Presbyterian, Jewish, or Pagan hos-
pitals, if these existed.
The “ Young Victorian Patriotic League ”
has now been defeated in the two democratic
electorates (Oollingwood and Richmond) in which
it has tried to obtain a footing. The League’s
only result will be to confirm, in his suicidal poli-
tical faith, the poor, under-paid, snobbish clerk
who thinks that the man in moleskins is the
natural enemy of the man in a paget coat.
The burden of the present Griffilwraithiau
sophistry is, that the Kanaka is to be strictly
confined to field-work on sugar-plantations, and
that employers who otherwise use him are to be
subject to very severe penalties. Bunkum ! One
set of politicians is already claiming that maize-
farmers should be allowed the same Kanaka
privileges as sugar-growers; another still more
candid set contend that all employers should bo
permitted to employ Polynesians as they please.
“Freedom of contract!”
Query : Should an Attorney-General (who
may be real or acting-Premier into the bargain)
be permitted to hold retaining fees from persons
involved in civil actions that clearly involve
questions of fraud and criminal conspiracy?
Ought not politics and the administration of
justice to be completely divorced from each other?
In short, should not the Attorney-Generalship be
a non-political office, held by the most suitable
man “during good behaviour,” in the same way as
judicial appointments are held ?

Why is it that, when the daily paper man
is reporting the mcetiugMff the candidate I.is editor
doesn’t like, that candidate is always greeted with

The Queensland Governor’s speech, spoken
into the vice-regal phonograph by the Griffil-
wraith : (1) Tears for the late Clarence; (2) a
Kanaka whoop; (3) a land-grant-railways plea;
(4) a meat-export bribe; (5) economy cant; ((»)

Graham Berry got a bad reception at
Brunswick and was hooted down. The Age is
half inclined to support Berry this time, so it
points out that the row was caused by an organised
crowd of rowdies from Collingwood, who crowded
out the genuine elector. If the genuine elector
had been able to get in he would have cheered, of
course, but he didn’t get a show. He is always
getting left that way. The genuine elector, by
the way, is the man who votes the way we want.
The organised rowdy from Oollingwood is the
other man.
The N.S Wales Legislative Council has
thrown out the Early Closing Bill by 25 votes to 7.
This is about the only commendable thing which
the Council has done this year. N.S. Wales
doesn’t want a Bill to make any man close his
shop when he doesn’t feel that way ; what it wants
is a measure to prevent him working any employe
more than eight hours per diem, and when that is
provided for, the boss should be allowed to keep
open all day and all night, and thereby furnish
employment for three separate shifts of workers,
if he is so inclined. The rejection of the Early
Closing Bill doesn’t matter —but if- the Council
rejects the Eight Hour Bill when it comes along,
then it ought to be made to feel as if all Gehenna
had broken loose.
In Bananaland Legislative Council, the
Address-in-Reply to the Griffilwraith Kanaka and
Land-Grant-Railway Speech from the Throne,
was moved by Dr. Marks, one of Griffith’s
tamo admirers, and E. B. Forrest, formerly a
Mcllwraithian when to be such was to be a
Griffithphobist, and always a Kanakophile. Its
first adverse criticism came from Billy Brookes,
erstwhile Griffith’s colleague in representing
North Brisbane, when G. was posing as aQueeus-
land Wilberforce. Billy owes bis seat in the
Council to Griffith, but remains solid against
Kanakas. The attitudes of the three Councillors
named, form one of many contemporaneous
incidents which grimly illustrate how “mixed”
party politics have become in Queensland.
The British India mail steamer from Eng-
land arrived on the Queensland coast the other
day, and peacefully dumped ashore 134 more
paupers to add to the starving unemployed. It
would have been about as kind an act if it bad
turned them adrift on a raft in mid-ocean. What
a fearsome load of curses there must be on Grif-
fith’s head—the curses of the people whom he has
brought out to howl for food in a strange land,
and to sleep in dust-bins and against lamp-posts',
and to wander around asking for work where
there is no work to be had, and finally to die
anyhow and to bo buried or left unburied accord-
ing to circumstances ! If we were Griffith, when
the Resurrection comes, we would lie still and
take no notice lest we should meet some of them.
“ The outlook iu Europe ” is so disgrace
fully peaceful at present that the German military
authorities have, it is cabled, decided to declare
Alsace in a state of siege whenever war may break
out. A more obvious attempt to incite a row was
never made by men in buckram. It was open to
German war-mongers to do what they liked in
Alsace in the event of a decent excuse being avail-
able, and to preserve a discreet silence on the sub-
ject until that time arrived. But in the interests
of bloodshed these clanking brawlers are talking
fight, and doing their level best to stir up the
slumbering animosity of France by Act of Parlia-
ment. There would seem to bo very little grit in
German socialism, after all. As soon as a
decorated drone flourishes his sword and raves
about the Fatherland, all the apostles of peace
and progress swallow their principles and grunt
for joy.
The Bulletin portrait of Deeming, as ho
appeared in Darlinghurst Gaol, was reproduced
by Melbourne Herald without acknowledgement.
Queerly enough, The Bulletin, the only paper
which succeeded in obtaining the much-desired
photo, in question, got it through a Melbourne
source.
If the portrait of Williams’ late cemented
relative, as it keeps on appearing in the daily
papers, can be proved to be anything like what
the lady really was when alive, then her bereaved
husband ought to get off. Both Williams’ wives,
in fact, as depicted in the press, look like females
that would have to be married under the influence
of chloroform.
“ Gustav Jovanovitch, the greatest cattle-
breeder in Russia, and called the ‘King of the
Steppes,’ owns 000,000 acres of land, and possesses
more than a million sheep and 34,000 shepherd
dogs.” That means 34 dogs to every 1200 sheep,
putting the sheep down roughly at 1,200,000. We
never shepherded in Russia, but we know what
would happen to a shepherd in Australia if ho
started after a flock of 1200 sheep with 34 dogs.
A deadbeat the other day, at Redfern
Railway .Station, asked an innocent-looking indi-
vidual, whom he had never seen before, for the
loan of a shilling “till to morrow.” The innocent-
looking individual stared open-mouthed at him in
astonishment, and, after recovery, merely said
“ Thank you.” “ What for ?” asked the deadbeat.
“For the information.” The deadbeat requested
an explanation. The innocent-looking one said
tliat for some time he had been much puzzled by
the doubt whether lie was a bigger fool than ho
looked, or looked a bigger fool than he was, and
that the deadbeat had very obligingly settled the
question. The information was undoubtedly
worth a shilling, but, onlheother hand, the dead-
beat had seriously hurt his vanity by the implied
reflection on his physiognomy.. Moreover, by
keeping the shilling, he would be teaching the
deadbeat the folly of judging by appearances.
The coin wasn't forthcoming.
Stead has been persistently booming tho
much-puffed electro-homccopathic remedies of the
Italian Count Mattel, who claims empirically to
cure, amongst other ailments, influenza and
cancer. The perusal of a few snips from this
person’s charlatanry should be sufficient for most
people. Amongst a lot of other directions auent
the treatment of influenza:—
Take Febrifngo No. 1 in the second dilution, alter-
nately, with a melange of Angioiticd 3, Pectorale 1, and
Canceroso 1, in the second dilution, a teaspoonful every
ten minutes.
Two drops of white electricity (!) in a dessert-spoonful
of water every hour till the fever is reduced.
White electricity spoused over the forehead will greatly
relieve the acute pains nearly always felt in that region.
Asa preventive against infection, take five globules of
Scrofoloso Giaponne dry on the tongue night and
morning.
“ White electricity !” Bah ! Bring us some blue
lightning, iced, frosted over with green ether,
frozen solid ; a bat’s sweetbread, the dorsal fin of
a platypus, the caul of a bunyip, the smoked
duodenum of a rattlesnake, the off-hind kidney of
a gohanna, the gall-bladder of a frog, all bashed
into a melanye of snails’ jelly, and impregnated
with three actinic beams from the demon-star.
Algol.

“ The police think Deeming possesses the
/are faculty of altering his facial expression at
will.” After the publication of this in all the
daily papers, what a farce will be the ceremony of
having the prisoner identified by the Melbourne
tradesmen !.
# # *
Dear Bulletin, —Why on earth should we
have only “ detective diagnosis ” in this Deeming
ease ? Doctors who have seen sham epileptics
(not at all uncommon in the army and navy)
are procurable, and can dress as watchers. If poor
Victoria can’t afford that, let Detective Cawsey
put soap in Deeming’s eye the next time he has a fit.
If he screws the eye violently up, he is shamming,
and every good doctor can swear it. Surely some-
thing like this will be done. And let the watchers
be forbidden to gratify the struggling Deeming
(i.c., if he is shamming) by holding him. In hos-
pital we stretch them on the floor, and allow the
air to do the wrestling—a much more humane
plan.—Yours truly, M.D.
* * *
London Daily News says that valuable
results will be obtained from the moral anatomisa-
tion which will go on during Deeming’s trial.
True : it will lie found, should the prima-facie
case be elaborated to conviction, that in Mr.
“Williams” is reincarnated the soul of Henry
VIII.
* * #
“ Detective Cawsey has also found amongst
D eeming’s things a new wedding-ring, which has,
however, been slightly used. This is belu ved to
have been the one worn by the murdered woman
at Windsor, and intended for Miss Ronnsevelle.”
Don t shudder, ladies. When you marry a
widower you never can be sure that the ring he
gives you has not been manufactured from the
gold plate of his deceased wife’s teeth, stolen from
her mouth after death by the undertaker.
The alleged Windsor murderer made Ids
greatest mistake in select’ug police-ridden W. Aus-
tralia as the scene of future operations. Another
big mistake was that made by The Bulletin in
omitting to print a long article written by its
valued contributor “ Dipso.,” who, during the
police-search for “ Williams,” proved to his own
satisfaction that the man wante-1 would probably
make for West Australia.
# # #
Several shopkeeporsin a Melbourne suburb
are haunted by a certain young lady whews addicted
to dropping into their emporiums at all hours of the
day and telling what she knows about “the
monster” (Deeming) in a loud voice across the
counter. For a time the story was interesting
and good for trade, but since all the customers
have heard it three or four times, and feasted
their eyes to repletion upon the fair young
narrator, there is apt to be a hasty retreat of the
public when she comes along with the same old
lot of particulars on the tip of her dear little
tongue. Wherefore the shopkeepers in question
begin to regret the Windsor tragedy, and all the
revelations hanging to it.
# # #
Marshall Lyle, engaged todefend Deeming,
has been the most puffed and paragraphed solicitor
in Melbourne for some months past. But how
came it that Deeming wanted to hire anybody for
such a notorious job? He should have put his
case up to auction, with a thumping reserve on it.

Apropos the fact that Deeming appears
to have removed his moustache with a piece of
glass bottle, the South Sea, Islanders employ the
same makeshift. The idea is adopted from Job,
who took him a hunk of potsherd to scrape him-
self withal. However, there are pretty good
reasons for believing that Mr. Deeming is not the
man to endure the reproaches of .his wife as long
as Job did.

Chief Justice Darley, it is announced, will
preside, at Deniliquin Circuit Court, to be held
next week, in place of Justice Windeyer, who in
ordinary course would have taken this circuit.
The Bulletin wonders if this alteration has been
made because the man on whose evidence the
Frenchman, Cordini, tried before Judge Windeyer,
for alleged murder, was hanged, is to be tried for
alleged attempted murder. It will be remembered
that Cordini strongly protested bis innocence-
even on the scaffold—and that his solicitor and
counsel both impugned the fairness of his trial.

Theauthorities of a certain Western N.S.W.
Corporation Saleyards are very particular indeed.
When their attention is drawn to any cancerous
beast, the animal is not sold as fat beef; oh, dear
no ; it is “ bought after the sales and killed in the
presence of the police.” Then “the cancerous
portion is destroyed by fire, and the health))
portion (?) given to the pigs.” What price, now,
Western-fed pork ?
# # #
For ten years past, the average fatalities
from snake-bite in Victoria were four per annum.
That was before the infallible strychnine cure was
discovered. Now there seems to be a boom in
snakes, and already this season sixteen persons,
all of whom would otherwise have died, have been
saved by means of the new remedy. The situa-
tion is almost this. A person is bitten by a snake
—in nine cases out of ten a harmless animal of no
account at all. Then sundry kind friends seize
upon him and fill him up with brandy till he is
fearfully and horribly drunk. When the doctor
arrives he simply diagnoses him as a person in a
comatose condition, which he certainly is, and
saves him by a strychnine injection, lie wakes
up with a much enlarged head and all the other
signs of having been out on a tear, and these are
set down as the after-effects of snake-bite. The
idea of enquiring about the snake never occurs to
anybody.
An exciting scene took place at a fashion"
able Sydney boarding-house on a recent Saturday
night. A Hebrew money-lender who had a bill-
of-sale over the furniture swooped down with a
couple of delivery-vans and dragged the beds from
under the astonished boarders, who were left at
two a.rn. in a furnitureless house. A pelting rain-
storm was raging at the time, and into this the
furniture was bundled amid the howls of the land-
lady and'the boarders.
* • #
In one cabin of the Ormuz (just arrived
from England) four girls came to Australia to be
married. No. 1, on reaching Albany, had to be
introduced to her intended; she had quite for-
gotten him —not having seen him for six years.
In five minutes she broke off the engagement.
She goes back to ma per first mail steamer. At
Adelaide, No. 2 leaned over the steamer’s side,
and nestling against a certain valiant sea-dog, she
simply ignored the expectant bridegroom. No. 3
sprang joyfully into her hero’s arms at Melbourne;
No. 4 did ditto in Sydney. Woman is constant-
-50 per cent, of her. The Ormuz has the record
for the coming brides’ list. A couple of years ago
she brought a consignment of seven to Sydney
alone.
# # #
The only regretable feature of the sale of
the Hay-Berry estate (Shoalhaven district,
N.S.W.) was that £lOO,OOO had to be raised in
order to be sent out of the province as a donation
to St. Andrew’s University, Scotland, in accord-
ance with the will of the deceased millionaire,
Berry. Much gratuitous heat has been wasted
over the prophesied injustice which threatened
the tenants, who would be compelled to quit their
holdings and abandon their improvements with-
out a chance to secure the freeholds of the lands
they had learned to look upon as their life
portions; but it must now be admitted that
Mr. Hay has acted, in bush phrase, “like a
white man.” With one exception, all of his
tenants have been allowed to acquire the free-
holds of their ground. In several instances ho
actually stopped the competition in favour of
the bidding tenant.
Please turn it up, and send us no more rot, man
vVe saw her out, last Sunday, with the potman,.
The jury in the Bird o’ Freedom libel-
case in Sydney last week, returned with this find-
ing Verdict for the plaintiff; damages, one
farthing ; each party to pay their own costs.” The
Court smiled at the last order, but let the jury go
without snorting at the breach of judicial
privilege. As the N.S.W. law (Reids Act
stands, n plaintiff who fails to get 40s, damages
does not get a costs-certificate.

Burdett Smith, M.L.C., has hinted that
an English syndicate representing two millions
sterling has been formed with the express objeqt
of exploiting Sydney while city and suburban
properties are “down” in value. If so, this
means the cruel result of a subsequent artificial
boom'with its consequent horrors of collapse.
# # »
At Orange (N.S.W.) a man named John
Jupp has been committed for trial for shooting an
individual who came round and “ tin-kettled ”
him on his wedding-night. We can’t remember,
just at this moment, whether .Jupp killed the
other man or not, but if that obnoxious person is
really dead, or even horribly disfigured, The
Bulletin seriously contemplates opening a sub-
scription for the prisoner. The man who goes
round to another man’s house and there makes a
deafening clamour on a kettle, or vociferates on a
kerosene-tin, is a public enemy, a clown, and
a maniac, and the person who shoots him
deserves well of his country. When the law
doesn’t protect a man then he has to protect him-
self, and as there is no statute that deals in any
efficient manner with the dancing dervish who
makes night horrible at his friend’s keyhole, Jupp
did the only thing that anv reasonable man could
do. Hi s methods, no doubt, were somewhat
crude—a person of a more collected mind would
have invited the tin-kettle person in to have some
refreshment, and then secretly poured boiling-
water over him and cemented him under the
hearthstone; but it is probable that Jupp did the
best he could under the special circumstances of
tile case.
* # #
A Eurasian masher, who has been on a
visit to Fremantle, W.A., has eloped with a
society blossom who is closely related to “all the
fust fam’lies ” of the Cinderella colony, and whose
parents are wealthy. The lovers are half-way to
the Eurasian’s native Bombay by this time, and
have managed to leave behind them a report that,
before their departure, they were married by
special license. This marriage, by the way, recalls
a case in which a rich Chinese married (in Vic-
toria) a sister of the wife of a haughty potentate,
who moved in the most “ utter’’ circles. It quite
broke up that potentate to be asked by all the
little street-boys concerning the health of his
brother-in law.

day up to Mrs. (fuest, at home, in a Melbourne
suburb, and got £3 for splints, etc., for Mr, Guest,
who had met with a shocking accident. Mr.
Guest came home all right: his benevolent friend
got away with the £3.

* # *
The latest bowled-out “lord,” arrested for
deserting his family in Australia, turns out to be ft
hair-dresser. For some reason that has never
been sufficiently accounted for, nearly every
bogus peer turns out ih the end to be a hair-
dresser or else a valet. Either the average noble-
man is very like a shampooer or a flunkey, or else
the shampoo-man looks just like a British duke*
and they both much resemble a footman. It is
Very rough on the old nobility either way. The
present hairdresser had the run of the vice-regal
box at the theatre where he appeared with
the distingnised Order of the Bath upon his
noble chest, and was entertained by all the
clubs, and was shown over the war-vessels in the
harbour, and even got the entree at Government
House. And now —! “ Shave or hair-cut, sir?”

The collapse of St. Joseph’s Building
Society (Sydney) appears to have come about
through a certain pious and kind-hearted fuzziness
on the part of everybody concerned. There seems
to have been no actual embezzlement, no inten-
tional fraud, no desire to do wrong to anybody-
only everybody concerned was a warm-hearted
Irishman, and he threw- the accounts off his chest,
in a manner of speaking, instead of keeping them
in the ordinary business way. When any borrower
failed to pay his instalments good old St. Joseph
would let him slide and allow him to live rent-free
for ages before it hunted him up." It never pur-
sued a debtor with any vigour. It lent money
before the mortgage was prepared, and, sometimes
never got any mortgage at all, and the securities
seem to have been kept in the safe, or in any
officer’s hind pocket, or behind the door, or any-
where. In short, it muddled away <£55,000 in a
cheerful, fat, benevolent way with an aroma of
whisky-and-water about it, that was eminently
THEM BONES.
“A perfect human skeleton weighted down
with stones has been found in the bush at Coola-
mon, N.S.W.”— Daily Paper.
We view the scene with dismal cyo,
We hear the victim gasp, “ Oh Lud !”
The curlew’s far-off, mournful cry,
The old, old, dull andsick’ning thud.
The dingo’s howl of fiendish glee,
The jackass’ deep, expansive gri t,
The same old crow on the same old tree -•
And a murky night ad lib . thrown ill.
And yet a grim, unholy mirth
Pervades our erstwhile solemn breast J
At last we feel one Joy on earth,
One tranquil thrill of perfect rest.
The sighing zephyrs did not play
Weird, mournful music o’er Ids hones ;
The wattles struck, he simply lay
Beneath those dull, prosaic stones.
J. S. S.
A BAR-BOY’S HOWL.
I love a barmaid ! Ne’er was passion rasher—
-0 ! help me, Bacchus, god of wine, to mash her !
Her name is Lily ; fair she is and chaste,
And scarcely twenty (leastways, round the waist).
In vain I struggle with my strange emotion
1 cannot eat, I’m kept alive by lotion ;
I haunt her pub. like any bony spectre,
In sly flirtations fearing to detect her.
But if, perchance, she rouses black suspicion,
She merely says that “ girls in her position
Must try to please e’en men they couldn’t care for”
(Yet, what does she let Spoffkins stroke her hair for’)
'Tis bitter sweet, this cross-the-countcr wooing
(Pour “ bob ” a day in sixpences I’m “ blooing ”),
Her depth of love I get no chance of testing,
For when she’s “off ” she swears she’s always “ resting.”
I think she’s true, and virgin pure, and tender
(.She wears six rings, hut those her cousins lend her,
Or so she says). My heart is very trusting.
I write these lines to save myself from busting.
THE BARD OF WOOLLOOMOOLOO.

been a happy institution which was always in a

* * *
An “ influential colonist” lias written the
Queen’s Private Secretary, to suggest that Prince
George of Wales should be called “Duke of Aus-
tralia ! ” We have before us the pamphlet issued
in Sydney by a crank who proposed, during
the Duke of Edinburgh’s visit to this country,
that he (Alfred) should be proclaimed King of
Australia. By the way. Sir Henry Parkes, in his
recent interview with a Pall Mall Gazette man
at Sydney, appears to have advanced arguments
which can only be regarded as pleas for the
ultimate establishment of a Royal family in Aus-
tralia.
» « #
The suggestion that George of Wales
should be created Duke of Australia, reminds us
a good deal of that American story in which the
leading character is the Duke D’Moses. Why. not
create George Duke D’Moses? It is a nice-looking
title and would sound well.
'* * #
A “ lord,” formerly of Sydney, has been
giving the Western country a turn. He had not
been there many hours ere he was voted a
“ splendid fellow.” A local squatter gave a ball
in his honor, and on the day following, at the
marriage of the local carpenter, his lordship con-
stituted himself best man and provided champagne
all round. His lordship’s bill for a couple of
weeks amounted to <£loo, chiefly for fizz ; he paid
it with promissory-notes at two months. After
borrowing <£lo from an old squatter to take him
on he got a merry send-off. He is now doing a
fortnight for vagrancy.
* # #
The Melbourne Postal authorities keep a
number of buggies, in which the postmen are
driven round, to empty the letter boxes. One of
the recent drivers, an old man, had a romantic
history. He and his brother were poor boys
together in Ireland, but the brother emigrated,
became a very wealthy Victorian (squatter, anti
died intestate, a bachelor, all the properly going
therefore to the poor man, who sailed for Melbourne
—and lost the pile in a few years.
* # #
A wise American dame has extracted from
a heap of statistics the discovery that nine-tenths
of the children of college-bred women survive
infancy, and this she considers to be convincing
evidence that a knowledge of the classics and a
good grounding in mathematics assist the produc-
tion of healthy babies. It is a pleasant, scholarly
theory for the sweet girl-graduates to swear by,
but infant mortality is a question of money all the
same. College-bred women are apt to many men
who can keep them in circumstances which-give
the little strangers a fine show to exist. The sur-
vival of the “ best off ” is, as the statisticians wdl
tell you, the great rule of baby lifo. Our Gracious
Queen gave nine pledges of affection to Saint
Albert, and most of her daughters have contri-
buted largely to llm population of palaces, but the
loss of a young plant has never happened to cast
a gloom over any of these Guelphio nurseries. In
many instances the infants were so sickly that the
cold wind of poverty would have withered them
in a fortnight, whereas careful treatment under
glass preserved them to the taxpayer and developed
them into comparatively healthy subjects for the
loyal illustrated newspapers.
* # •
Never trust an elderly, bow-legged man in
Australia; all bow-legged men are not convicts
but nearly all old convicts have bow-legs. Don't
rely too much on a knock-kneed man ; the convicts
who are not bow-legged are mostly knock-kneed
instead—it all depends on which angle their legs
gave way at. Also, don't place implicit confidence
in a man who is furnished with straight legs—as
likely as not he has assumed them by way of a
disguise.

We hear that the directors of the W. A,
company which engaged Swans ton as mine-
engineer, and who organised nice little dinners
and balls and parties in honour of the alleged man
of cement, are not anxious to have their names
published in this connexion.
* # *
“Swauston,” while in custody in West
Australia, was treated as an accuse of considerable
importance. Ho was dieted from outside on an
elaborate scale, and was allowed to write to the
press on his local grievances. He had the greatest
possible objection to bo sketched or photographed,
and in Court, during the preliminary hearings, his
only trouble seemed to be to dodge the amateur
and his “infernal apparatus.”

Archbishop Dunne, of Wilcannia (N.S. W.),
means to have the debt on the Broken Hill R.C.
church speedily liquidated. He lias, we read,
tinri" up in the church a list of all the church-
members, whom ho has taxed as follows :
Female servants in receipt of wages, .£1; single
men do., .£1 10s. ; married men with a family,
£1; propertied folk, from £2 up to £lO, accord-
ing to esoteric gauging. All dues thus scheduled
are to be paid within four months. Under pain
of —what ?

Williams seems to have served as a soldier
somewhere or other. The man accused of murder

medal amongst his personal effects.
• * *
He was a N. Queensland magnate who only
liquored in the best society. At the fifth whisky
he always told the same story of his uncle, Sir
Hugh Brose, of Edinburgh, who was so much
respected that the general public always took off
their hats when he loomed up on the horizon. A
stranger who knew the anecdote sauntered in and
•was introduced to the nephew. “ ‘ Brose, Brose ’ !
I know that name ; any relative of Sir Hugh who
was so much re ?” “Yes! yes! my uncle.
Landlord, bring in champagne.”
* # #
At one of the most dreary-looking spots in
Queensland a traveller pulled up at a shanty with
the intention of having a drink. Out of a shed
came a man who grabbed at a water-bag, and was
shoved out of sight in short order. The traveller
went his thirsty way. A week later he heard
that Paul T , one of the whitest men that ever
L’ENFANT TERRIBLE.
“Ma, is that kitchen butter in the ba[?]ket?”
went West, had died suddenly at the shanty.
They brought in a verdict of “ epilepsy.”
* * #
Bruce, playing for Victoria against the
Englishmen, hit a fiver into the members’ pavilion.
The ball was seen to strike a lady, and on Bruce
going over to apologise, he found that the lady
was his wife. Wonder what he said? Some
cricketers would have said : “What did you get
in the road for? It would have been a sixer, if
you hadn’t stopped it.”

Dr. Bingham Crowther, who was remanded
at Hobart P.C. pending the discovery of the prin-
cipal witness against him, is now practising again.
# * *
The other day a young man, in issuing a
card asking the shareholders of a certain
company to elect him as a director, mentioned
thereon that he was “the son of So-and-So.”
Grimly remarked the old man, on seeing the card,
“ So far as I know.”
* # * *
A well-recognised, Australian industry is
“ jury-stiffening”— i.c ., jury-“squaring” or brib-
ing. They “all do it.” Talk to an attorney on
the steps of the Supreme Court about a case that
is going on inside, and lie will very likely tell you,
if he knows you pretty well, that it’s all right—
he lias “stiffened ” one or two of the jury, or he
has reason to suspect that they have been stiffened
by the man on the other side. It is no secret that
there are men in the Australias' who get their
living, and good livings, too, by “ squaring ’
jurors. One of the best-known practitioners in this
department of science is Mr. Joe . “ Why
don t we give the rest of his name?” Simply
because he would stiffen the jury empannelled to
try the libel-action.

The South Melbourne Salvationists have
been in a dreadful flurry over the disappearance
of their lady captain, well-known in Sydney,
because of a contraband, though innocent, love
affair.

“ Drifting ” is the title of the Australian
tale written by Mrs. Grimes, wife of “ Jemmy ”
Grimes, late of Toowoomba. It is being brought
out by Gordon and Gotcli.
# # #
Australians seem to hanker after the life
of a country gentleman, writes The Bulletin's
London correspondent, probably because they can
make a much better show for their money than in
town. W. O. Gilchrist has taken Oakley Hall,
near Basingstoke, where Mr. W. C. Cooper once
resided. Mr. Charles Parbury is settled at The
Down House, near Gloucester, and Mr. Robert
Lucas Tooth-mark the “Lucas,” please—dates
his letters from Foliejon Park, Windsor.
# * #
Many of the unemployed who sleep in
Sydney Domain get back-numbers of the local
dailies and use them as bed-clothes. Man loves a
dry and sleep-producing bed.

Melbourne Licensed Victuallers complain
of the hardness of the times. No wonder, when
the liquid-haters are so busy.

Parson Middleton, at North Shore
(Sydney) Wesleyan Church, the other Sunday
night, preaching of the moral influence of poets
on human nature, compared Tennyson to chloride
of lime! The good pastor has evidently been
reading up the Windsor murder.
# # #
Dear Bulletin, —In re your article on
leprosy. For years I’ve not permitted the buy-
ing of vegetables from Chinamen, preferring to
pay a little more to the white man. Last week our
greengrocer coolly let out that as he was short of
vegetables he had to buy some from a Mongol.
It seems there’s no evading the yellowskins in any
way; so, if we must have leprosy, let us have it
on the cheap. Apropos, one does not hear much
about the disease being prevalent in those cities of
America which swarm with Chinese. —Yours
truly, Lakomai. [Don’t we, indeed ! The lepers
of Chicago and San Francisco are among the
regular sights, to anyone who knows his way
about those cities.—Ej). B.]

It was only natural that the coroner’s jury
at Rainhill should censure the police for not dis-
covering Mr. Deeming's murders at the time.
Through this negligence the poor old “ mother
country ” lost one of the spiciest trials of modern
times.

will, bequeathing ,£50,000 a year to the Duke of
Teck and family. By the way, it isalso rumoured
that the sorrowing May Teck shows symptoms of
animation in the society of her late sweetheart’s
brother George.

post as a sop to the wealthy classes. Many mem-
