The announcement that the associated
banks of Melbourne have made arrangements to
stand by one another in tin; hour of need is signifi-
cant. But the wisdom of suggesting more panics
from the house-tops, so to speak, is decidedly
questionable.
» * •
Dear Bulletin, — Your article on “ In-
spectors of Agriculture” brings to mind an old
Victorian farmer, whose son passed a first class
exam, at the Dookie Agricultural College. A
friend offered congratulations, and remarked that
he supposed Bob would soon be home to help him
manage the farm. The old man stood aghast at
the idea, and energetically replied, “No blanky
fear.” “Well, what are you going to do with
him ?” asked the friend. “ Make him a Professor
of Agriculture, with a good Government screw,”
said the old chap.—Billycan.
# * *
The shareholders of the Centennial Land
Bank (Melbourne) found some gratifying informa-
tion waiting for them at their last annual meeting.
The “ bank ” was originated in the boom period,
and while that joyous time was on it used to buy
land in all directions, regardless of consequences.
Now its chief business is to get rid of the same
land, also regardless of consequences. It bought
one estate for £115,000, paying £45,000 cash and
giving bills for the balance, and now in its old age
it has given the land back gratis to the original
proprietor, and let him keep the £45 000, on con-
dition that he returned the promissory notes and
allowed the painful subject to drop. It paid
£lO,OOO on another property and gave bills for
£24.000, and now it feels thankful to lose the
£lO,OOO and the land and have nothing more said
about it. The directors are also making efforts to
recover debts due to the bank ; they spent £2O in
selling up a man who owed them £2OOO, and the
net proceeds of the “estate ” were £2 Os. Bd. It
pays better to be a coffee-stall than a bank in
these times.

• “No wise man ever found a dead man.”
Similarly, if you find two people together “ under
compromising circumstances,” say nothing about
it —if you don’t want to go to gaol.
* * *
What kills the average new daily paper in
the long run ? The insane craze for an eight-page
sheet. A new eight-page paper on the average
eats its own head off in waste paper and use! ss
type-setting, and goes to its grave amid the
curses of its shareholders.
# # %
If there be anything in leprosy contagion,
what a fertile disseminator a leprous letter-carrier
must be ! A Rockhampton (Q.) postman is a sus-
pected leper.
# # *
A Northern parson who chimes in with the
slave-importers, is finding that the way of the
nigger advocate is hard. Press correspondents
are saying very nasty things—amongst others,
that the parson sells his photos, to Ids Kanaka
lambs at a handsome profit.

A post-office official “got the sack ” in an
Australian capital the other day for being short
in his cash. His salary was £(3O per annum, his
age 31, and he had charge of over £2OO sterling.
* * »
Judge Chubb (N.Q.), declined to try the
malpractice case against Dr. Hunter Finlay, of
Townsville, because one of the persons concerned
in it had at one time been in his service.

Miss Lilley, eldest daughter of the Chief
Justice of Queensland, is to be married with
Alexander Cran, of Wide Bay, Q., tarly in May.
# # #
It is now about time that Manly bad some
other means of communication with Sydney than
by steamer. One day the stearing-gear of one of
the boats will go wrong between the Heads in a
heavy beam sea, and the boat will drift on to the
cliffs and be smashed like an eggshell, to the utter
wiping* nit of 200 or 300 Manlyites. Manly will
then be the black beast of the insurance-societies
and will be better stocked with widows than any
other place in the county of Cumberland.
# # #
The most urgent needs of the Woolloo-
raooloo and other Sydney suburban swimming-
baths, with the exception of those at Manly, are
disinfecting rooms and a deodorising apparatus
for tiie use of persons befouled by fetid swims.
Why should people swim in sewage?

ital of the State. The sheriff is a

‘ ‘ eral years in my family and for my
* ‘ own use, and found it does me
more good than any other remedy.

‘ ‘ call Sick Headache, A pain comes
‘ ‘ in the back part of my head first,
and then soon a general headache
until I become sick and vomit.

“ after eating, a pressure after eating
“at the pit of the stomach, and
‘ ‘ sourness, when food seemed to rise

“pepsia, &c.” - f ®

ÜBLIC NOTICE
BEDDING.
There is perhaps no part of House-Furnishino
as to the genuineness and value of which the publi
are so entirely dependent upon the nraerL>„i
knowledge and a
COMMERCIAL MORALITY
of vendors as
BEDOIKTGr,
and certainly the importance and the necessity for
pure, suitable, and properly manipulated materials
cannot be overrated.
A ricketty and badly finished article of Furni.
ture may annoy, and an ill-designed sideboard or
cabinet offend good taste ; but, even then no
serious harm occurs. But with improperly nur’ifioß
or badly made BEDDING, how different the tale
for then you have that which is INJURIOUS
to HEALTH, and undoubtedly SPREADS
DISEASE.
See report on Typhoid Fever and other diseases
in Sydney and suburbs from 1876 to 1891.
See Census and Industrial Returns for 1891.
COMPARATIVELY FEW FURNITURE
WAREHOUSES IN SYDNEY
manufacture the bedding they sell, but, buying
from wholesale makers, have to offer their cus-
tomers Made-up Goods, of the contents of which
they actually know nothing.
Let us consider for a moment that one-third of
one’s life is spent in bed. Does it not concern you
to know that so great a part of life’s short span
ought to be carefully and properly provided for ?
Have you ever taken the trouble to examine
your mattress to find out what is in it? Whether
HORSEHAIR or HOG HAIR,
WHITE JAVA KAPOK,
OR ONLY THAT
VERIEST OF RUBBISH,
THE OLD BLACKRAG FLOCK,
Or that short, seedy Indian Kapok called
Cotton Fly ?
Be wise, be wise. Do not let another day pass
until you have thoroughly examined your bedding.
Those Drapers, Ironmongers, Co-operative
Societies, and others who simply take orders for
bedding from the
PRICE LIST OF CHEAP MAKERS
are still worse off, having no knowledge whatever
of its manufacture, and are governed simply by
CHEAP PRICE LISTS,
and in many instances just place the order with
whatever wholesale maker offers them the greatest
discount.
Nor is this the only disadvantage their customers
suffer, as too frequently the bedding is supplied
by small makers, who have neither adequate
machinery to prepare the materials nor suitable
premises in which to carry on the work. Indeed,
if the purchasers of bedding could only see the
condition and surroundings of some of the makers
of bedding they would not allow the bedding to
come within coo-ee of their backyards, let alone
their bedrooms.
LAWLERS, of GOB George-street, Brickfield-
hill, have for many years manufactured all Bedding
upon their OWN PREMISES, importing the
materials DIRECT, employing the most improved
machinery, and having the work turned out under
their own supervision. We therefore can
GUARANTEE UNPRECEDENTED
VALUE
and Unrivalled Purity in whatever Bedding or
Upholstery leaves our steam factories. The enor-
mous increase in our Bedding and Wire Mattress
Department is perhaps the best proof that
LAWLER’S
efforts to supply only pure and wholesome and
healthy bedding at moderate and popular prices
have been thoroughly appreciated.
The Manufactories ADJOIN the Warehouse in
George street (608), and customers are invited to
see the machinery in motion and witness the pro-
cess of manufacture.

No notice will be taken of communications which arrive
at this office insufficiently stamped.
“ The Breaker ” : You have been anticipated in

the ointment with Mary was her horrid colonial twang.”

write to one of the leading firms—Paling’s or Nicholson’s,

President of N.S.W. Legislative Council occupies a little
room “into which he cannot invite any distinguished
visitors,” only again reminds us that Cupid has “ distin-
guished visitor” on the brain... “E. Morton”: The
genuine “Jack the Ripper” undoubtedly wrote his
capital “ Js” in French fashion... “ Gripman Thanks...

paid ? In New York the ordinary policeman begins at £4
weekly, and distinguished service is liberally rewarded
. . “Reader”; One-man-one-vote means one vote, no
more, no less, for every sane man outside gaol, whether
he can read and write or not “H.J.K.”: (1) He is
English, he lived in Australia several years as a journalist.
(2) It has not yet been republished, but soon will be ..

hero goes away to become a great author, and comes back
a leper, with a roll of MS. pressed against his infected
chest. Do you mean that leprosy and authorship go
together, or what? “H. Montague: An eminently
surprising story, but if we stated in print that a man
walked into the Q.N. Bank, Brisbane, and threw snakes
at the manager till that gentleman granted him an un-
secured overdraft, we would be charged with undermining
the financial institutions of the country “Smiler II.” :

unduly rough on Gehenna “ A.M.S.” (Melbourne):

quit the business “T. Davenport” (Sj'dney) : A

Brigalow Mick.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
A dandy old horseman is Brigalow Mick -
Which liis name, sir, is Michael O’Dowd
Whatever he’s riding:, when limber is thick.
He is always in front of the crowd.
A few tangled locks that are fast turning white
Crown a physog. the colour of brick.
But as keen as a kestrel’s -as bold and as bright
Is the blue eye of Brigalow Mick.
lie is Martin’s head-stockman, on Black-Cattle Creek
All the boys there are rare ones to lide
But Mick is the “ daddy and far you may seek
Ere you find such an artist in hide.
lie’ll turn out a halter, or stockwhip can make,
As you’ve seldom cast eyes on before ;
And never the “nugget” was calved that could break
Michael’s whips, which he plaits by the score.
All the lads on the station are handy enough,
Nor are frightened of grafting too hard,
But Mick, if the cattle are rowdy and rough,
Is the pick of ’em all in a yard.
A bad colt to tackle—a mad one to steer
Through thick timber—you’ll hear Martin boast—
Mick yet is unrivalled, there isn’t his peer
Bight from Camooweal in to the coast.
Ay ! long may it ho ere the scrubs are bereft
Of the clearskins that give us the spoi t,
And long may the station have stock-riders left, ■
Of the build of old Brigalow's sort.

A yam from “ below.” He was one of
a noted family of gold “ shakers ” (an old creed)
and got a job at a rich mine on a new field. At
crib-time he came “on top,” and hunted up the
manager. “Say boss, I’m full up, aint goin’ t’
work in this here blankety dash mine of yours.”
“ Hello ! what’s wrong—too wet for you ?” “ No,
but your gold’s too infernally fine.”
It was Willoughby Andrew, now serving a
life-sentence for murdering J. H. Bartlett, in
Sydney, who acted as agent when Frederick
Bailey Deeming, charged with the Windsor (Vic.)
and Rain hill (Eng.) murders and about 1100 other
crimes, assured his life with the A.M.P. Society
at Rockhampton in 1883. Deeming’s then recorded
height was 5ft. Sin. According to his Darlingburst
Gaol record it is 5ft. 4iin.
A Hughenden (Q.) man sat in his private
office in converse with a clerk. In a half-opened
drawer, plainly v : sible to the clerk, was a bundle
of bank-notes. The employer was called out for a
few minutes. Not a soul entered the room during
his absence, but the clerk had stayed behind a
little while. When the employer returned, the
notes were missing. . . . Now, had this been
a case of life and death, circumstantial evidence
would have hanged that clerk, . . . A quarter
of an hour later, the employer was crossing his
backyard and saw a torn note—one of the lost
bunch—being blown along by the wind. In a
corner, a terrier pup was worrying the remainder,
which, however, were rescued in time to fully make
up the tally'.
The S.M. Herald recently set itself the
congenial task of proving that 1S.S.W. Protection
had locally raised the price of candles—“the light
of the poor”—by |d. per lb., while tallow, the
raw material of candles, had actually become
cheaper. This, of course, was the importers’ side
of the story. The Sydney Soap and Candle Co.
replied, showing that tallow had increased in cost
by £2 per ton, while the felling price of candles
had decreased —giving the Herald’s affirmatives
direct negatives. Further, that soap, a co-product
with candles, was now sold cheaper than last
year, in spite of See’s new duty of £3 per ton. In
an editorial foot-note, the Herald repeated its
mis-statements, and the Co. attempted to convince
the Foreign-trade journal of its error by pointing
out that it was quoting the i rice of an inferior
tallow utterly unsuitable for candle-making, and
offering to allow the Company’s business records
to be unreservedly inspected by any’ Herald
representative. This letter, the Herald, it is
claimed, declined to publish.
Dear Bulletin, — That German and
damper-yarn in a recent issue reminds me of a
little back-country (Barrier) incident a few years
ago. She was a boundary rider’s wife, and a
“ tough ’un ” on travellers ; he, an old stager of a
sundowner, and perhaps an equally hard case, had
passed the hut hungry once previously, and hadn’t
forgotten the circumstance. On this occasion he
possessed half-a-crown, and threw it to the two-
year-old youngster, which was amusing itself under
the bough shed ; he slung “Matilda ” off his back,
and leant across the rail, telling the child in a
loud voice what a beautiful little dear it was. Ma
soon appeared on the scene, and, seeing the half-
crown in the little darling’s fist, invited the S.D.
to stop and have a feed. After putting away
much damper and mutton, besides pocketing a
lump of brownie, he bade adieu to the hospitable
lady — grabbed the half-crown again as he
picked up his swag, telling the child that he
“thought it had been playing with the darned
money quite long enough,” and went on his way
rejoicing. He preferred walking another 12 miles
and keeping his half - crown to accepting her
invitation to stay for the night, but it wasn’t
meanness that prompted him to annex the coin
from the youngster, who had no idea of its
value —his sole object was to take the lady down
a peg, for which he was duly rewarded at the next
hut by a leg of mutton and a spell to do a day’s
washing, Fes, Dear Bulletin, I still have that
half-a-crown in my possession.— Tas. B. Maniac;

THE BEALE PATENT
HIGH ARM SEWING MACHINES
Have, by their continued merits and the general
satisfaction they have invariably given, won the
first place in public esteem, and are on all sides
admitted to be the best and most useful sewing
machines in the market.
FOR USE IN THE HOUSEHOLD
The Beale Machine presents innumerable advant-
ages. The mechanism and the adjustments are
perfect, while the working is most simple; and
the addition of all the most recent inventions and
improvements renders the manipulation of every-
thing connected with the machine a matter of
perfect ease. The result of this is, that with
ordinary care the machine will last for many
years, nothing can get out of order, and no time
is wasted in tiresome meddling with the parts.
It is a servant that will never grumble, never
rebel, never want nights out, willing even on
washing days. It only asks for a careful cleaning
now and then, a snug, dry corner and a little
genuine sewing machine oil. Any other oil will
make it sick, and dirt of all kind it abhors.
The Self-threading' Shuttle, of solid steel,
saves the trouble of threading by the end of the
cotton. It has an excellent tension, is strong and
simple.
The Bobbin Winder 1 is a marvel of in-
genuity, winding and guiding the cotton by
itself, as evenly as upon the reel whence it came,
and stopping of its own accord when the bobbin
is full.
For all kinds of family sewing and fancy the
Beale Machine has the greatest capacity.
Tne Improved High Arm. A great de-
sideratum with ladies has been the raising of the
arm of the machine, so as to allow the goods to be
sewn freely to pass underneath. This had to be
accomplished without increasing the weight of the
machine. In fact, the Beale runs with wonderful
ease and rapidity. It is almost silent, all clicking
sounds having been obviated. As is well known,
a good test of the finish of any mechanism is its
silence when in rapid motion.
With the Wonderful Beale Machine,
whether for hand or foot use, you can hem any
width, make double seams or fells, braid stitch
with chenille, Berlin wool, or gold cord, and such
like fancy work ; you may make a pretty lace
stitch, tuck over so finely, bind dresses, coats, or
carpets ; sew on trimmings without guiding, make
frilling, ruffling, or niching; quilt, weave your
own braid witli any combination of colours and
stitch it on at the same lime ; make thick, woollen
door-mats, embroidered antimacassais, window
curtains, and so forth ; you can easily and rapidly
perform the fashionable shirring or gathering so
popular for sleeves, bodies, or skirts of dresses
with an appliance supplied gratis ; lay the small
cords in hoods and stays ;do large cording; gather
with or without a band, and generally do all
kinds of straight sewing upon any material what-
ever. You can darn upon any woven goods, and
do crewel-work. The Beale Patent will pass
from eight folds of tweed or twenty of calico to
two of thinnest muslin, or even mosquito-net or
tulle, without the operator changing needle,
cotton, stitch, or tension, and the tine material
will not be gathered in the slightest. You may
make button and eyelet-holes, do over-casting,
make herring-bone stitch upon any materials.
This work is absolutely unique and can only be
accomplished upon the Beale Patent Machine.
Any of these performances will be shown at Gin-
shops at any time. The Beale Patent makes
a lock-stitch, which cannot ravel, yet by a momen-
tary alteration the sewing can be done so as to
rip from end to end, if desired. This peculiarity
is sometimes useful when children’s clothes are
intended to be altered.
Nickel-plating*. —In our climate the exposed
parts of the machine, and the applicants, need
to be protected from rust. We have, at consider-
able expense, added a handsomely nickeled wheel,
nickeled plates, screws, appliances, treadle-bar
and cones. Heightening the appearance, these
improvements also tend largely to preserve the
parts. This plating is imperishable.
A special feature of The Beale Machine is
that
ALL THE CABINET WORK
is made at our factory in Sydney from registered
designs, and of the choicest
AUSTRALIAN WOODS,
some of which are without doubt the moat beauti-
ful in the world. Suitability to the climate is
thus insured, and we are enabled to offer a greater
variety of styles, most handsome in design and
conveniently fitted for all the requirements of a
lady’s work-table.
These machines can be inspected at any time at
our principal show-rooms in Sydney, or at any of
our suburban and country branch shops, or from
travellers who are continually visiting all parts.
All the machines may bo had upon easy time-
payment, or for cash less three shillings in the
pound.

SPOUTING NOTIONS.
i Averages of the English 12 (in-
cluding Phillipson) in big matches totalled a little higher
than those of the representative dozen Australians.
Taking the four highest scorers on either side, there was
a difference of 1 run per man in favour of the visitors for
each innings—a fraction less than 37 against a fraction
less man 36. The remaining eight Britishers made a
general average of about 171 runs per innings, whilst the
next eight Australians (including Moses, who batted five
tunes, and Trott, who batted ten) made 161 runs. In
bowling, the best English quartette—Attewell, Peel,
Lohmann and Briggs—come out with a general average of
15.1 runs per wicket, as against the cost of 191 runs per
wicKet incurred by Worrall, Turner, M‘Leod, and George
Giffen collectively'. Worrall, however, bowled only half as
many balls as Trott, and not a third of Callaway’s total,
the two latter making bad averages of 31 and 35. On the
other hand, Sharpe (31 per wicket) bowled more than
Briggs or Peel. But if we take six bowlers for either
country Grace (27) and Sharpe as a set off against Trott
and Callaway the Britishers have still the best of it in this
department. In the face of all these figures the double
defeat of Grace & Co. seems inexplicable, for they scored
(on an average) a trifle more runs than the leading Aus-
tralians and bowled with much better results. The
wicket in the Adelaide match goes some way towards
explaining the riddle. Lumping the three “ test"
matches together, it appears that the Englishmen
(thanks in a great measure to the rain) had
an innings and 104 runs to the good, although they - lost
the Vet it has still to be explained how the
Englishmen s batting average goes only 1 run per man
higher than their opponents, whilst their bowling, in-
cluding 22 runs made off Stoddart, cost less by' 4 runs per
wicket. An industrious mathematician may' unravel this
mystery by the light of the fact that the Englishmen lost
only 97 wickets to bowlers in eleven-a-side matches,
whereas the Australians lost 153 wickets, “llunning-
out” was all in favour of our men. The visitors lost 13
of 110 wickets that way, and the Australians threw away
their lives in 7 only out of 160 instances. The Buu.etix’B
extract of statistics will be found a useful tonic for new
chums who are suffering from depressed spirits and
collapsed cricket expectations.
* • *
The one absolute failure of Sheffield’s team was
Sharpe, with bowling and batting averages, in first-class
matches, of 30 runs per wicket and 10 runs per innings.
In both departments the young Surrey cyclops came out
at the bottom of the list. Bean, with an average of 17
runs, was another expensive error, considering that he was
about the least valuable man in the field. Comparative
failures w'ere Briggs, M‘Gregor and Attewell, as batsmen,
and Peel as a bowler. But in truth none of the old
bowlers made as good averages as they have made against
Australian elevens in England, not even after allowing
for the harder w'iekets they get to work upon at
this end. Moreover, none of them looked like bowl-
ing quite as dangerously as of yore. Many yards
of stuff have been written about the doings of Lord Shef-
field’s defeated champions, but no one seems to have
commented upon the fact that they, like several teams
before them, show'ed to less advantage in Sydney' than in
Melbourne. N.S.W. has been able to trouble the’ English-
men, again and again, for y'ears past, whereas the Vic-
torians never seem to have a chance against them. This
being thus, the fact that Victoria maintains a slight
supremacy over N.S.W. in intercolonial matches, is
another proof of the “ glorious uncertainty of cricket,”
or perhaps it goes to show that the difference between
the Sydney and Melbourne climates is against the
Britishers.

Lord Sheffield’s proposal to bring out the flower
of British cricket solely for the purpose of playing three
test-matches, appears an acknowledgement that the pre-
sent English team is inferior to the All-Australian Eleven.
With champions Gunn and Shrewsbury added, the
Britishers would put our men fairly on their mettle, and
the only suggestion we have to offer is, that the date-
fixtures be left to meteorologist Wragge.
• * #
Mrs. W. G. Grace, in returning thanks for
floral tributes aboard the Valetta, said she was glad to
know that she would soon be back again with her children
in Australia. Lord Sheffield is a good friend to the
Graces. In default of getting W.G. with the contem-
plated “three match” team, he will, apparently, invite
the family to be his guests.
• # •
It is reported that English cricketers, Grace and-
Lohmann, had a little serious mill in Adelaide, a couple
of weeks ago. Later on, Grace insulted Stoddart and was
promptly “ plugged.” An exchange of blows again took
place between the last two on the morning of embarka-
tion per Valetta for London. It is known that in Sydney
one of the Englishmen was served with a writ of £5OO for
assault—in a row at the bottom of which was a barmaid.
Lord Sheffield “squared” the writ for £9O.

* # #
At a meat-freezing establishment on one of the
northern rivers 24 men recently formed themselves into a
syndicate to buy 24 tickets, as a joint speculation, in one
of the big sweeps. When the time came to put up the
money only' 23 turned up—the other member of the syn-
dicate being helplessly drunk and irresponsible. Then a
consultation was held ; the principal butcher suggested
that they should subscribe the missing member’s share on
his behalf and trust to him to repay them when he got
sober, and the proposition was seconded by the gentleman
who salted the hides ; while the tallow-boiler, seconded by
the man who skimmed the grease off the refuse-tank,
urged that they should eject him from the company' and
buy 23 chances only'. The amendment was carried, and
the syndicate bought tickets numbered (say) 90,000 to
Then ticket 90,023 scooped the big prize, and the
party just missed getting about £5OO each through not
taking the other ticket. And now the 23 who were sober
say that the man who was drunk ought to be hanged for
ruining the entire speculation, and he says that they
wrecked the business themselves by their meanness and
want of ordinary horse-sense, and the butcher and the
man who salts the hides are threatening the life of the
tallow'-boiler and the man who skims the fat off the tank,
and there will be murder done before the trouble is over.
• • #
Mr. J. O. Fairfax is especially wroth over the
defeat of his smart little yacht, Bui Bui, by Mr. S.
Hordern’s Bronzewing. Fife, of England, designed the
Bui Bui, and she was built here in Sydney'on Fife’s model.
Fife built the Bronzewing, and Mr. S. Hordern imported
her.

measure contributed to the failure of the recent tug-of-

of the contest,
* • •
Who ever heard of black Irishmen? Yet there
was a pure darkey in the Irish-Australian team in the
r ecent tug-of-war in Hobart.
* # #
The “poem” on the “Tenterfield Show” that
gained the first prize was a ropey bit of slaver. Here
are some viscous stalactites pulled up by the roots
In Sydney, last week, plaintiff in a libel-case
admitted that he ran ponies under his own name at
associated club meetings, and under somebody else’s name
at non-associatcd meetings. He “didn’t think it dis-
honest ”to do this. In Melbourne last week, it came out
in court that when an estate agent sold property to a man
who was one of his (the agent’s) partners, and also manager
of the banking co. for whom the estate was purchased,
£4BO coramiss on went into the private pockets of the
chairman and manager of the eo.’s board of directors,
the balance going to the members of the auctioneering
firm. Thus the man who aCtual'y bought for the banking
co. was, at one and the same time, the buyer, the seller,
an agent, and a manager. And, he “ did not see that it
was dishonest.”
The other day, the dead body of a work-
man was found in the Royal Park‘(Melb.). The
post-mortem proved that he had died of starvation;
evidence showed that he had been out of work for
months. “ The unemployed look too contented,”
lately said the Argus.
The effort in N.S.W. Legislative Council
to validate, by a side wind, the now celebrated
Tyson marriage, is handicapped in that con-
servative body by members’ conventional pre-
judices against the irregularity of the pair's
relations from the outset.

CADBURY'S COCOA is closely allied to
milk in the large proportion of flesh-forming and
strength-sustaining elements that it contains. It is pre-
pared on the principle of excluding the superabundance
of fatty indigestible matter with which Cocoa abounds

Tales of the Early Days.
No. IV.—The Convening of “The Ring.”*
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
BY PRICE WARUNG.
Captain Maconochie, who, with Major
Anderson, supplied the non-demoniac element in
the reigns of Norfolk Island Commandants, in
pursuance of his theory that the convict should be
encouraged to hope not alone for an alleviation of
his physical condition but also for “a new moral
nature,” hit upon an expedient for developing
even in hardened souls, those softening and refin-
ing tendencies which flow from a heart-felt solici-
tude for the welfare of others. By detaching
transports into groups, or “sub-gangs,” of three
or five members, and holding each man of the
three or five responsible for the good behaviour of
his comrades, an irresistible appeal was at once
made to the curiously-confused notions that
swayed the average convict mind. An argu-
ment which had regard only to their own
comfort or freedom from punishment would,
in the case of nine out of ten “old hands”
—the doubly-convicted convicts transported
from the mainland or Van Diemen’s Land—
be welcomed by an oath or a ribald jest. When
callousness in infamy was deemed to be an
honour —when irons were thought the insignia of
chivalry —and “connoisseurs in murder ” felt it a
privilege to take the hand of a “ looked-boot ”
victim —it was an insult to suggest an immunity
from penalties as a reason for right action. Take
another course, however, and appeal to the sense
of fraternity, which seldom died out even in the
“best” men, and that caution in conduct and
that eternal obedience to the regulations which a
“good ” man would never think of exerting in his
own interests, would be at once exercised for the
benefit of his group-mates. Most of Maconochie’s
attempts at penal reform sprang from his heart,
and were seldom based on a hard, logical appre-
hension of facts as they were. But, in respect to
his grouping system, his heart and his head acted
together. His judgment and his experience of
the “ old hands ’’ taught him that it was literally
and absolutely a point of honour with them
never to procure punishment for another trans-
port who manifested “spunk.” And his heart
showed him how to take advantage of this
characteristic. What “old hands” would not do
for themselves they should do for others. They
should respect regulations and official practices,
because violation of them would cause the infliction
of punishment upon their colleagues of the group.
# * #
Between the principal settlement (which was
supposed to change its name according to the sex of
the reigning sovereign, and, therefore, should
have been called Queenstown in the present reign,
but which, notwithstanding, was more often spoken
of as Kingstown than Queenstown) and the out-
lying barracks at Longridge, the Commandant
laid out, in March and April, 184—, a number of
farms of six to ten acres area each. On each farm
a hut or cottage was erected, and a company of
from three to five men was assigned to each hut
and farm. For the first year, no rental was
demanded by the Commandant. For succeeding
years each group of tenants had to contribute a
rental of twelve bushels of maize for each cleared
acre, this quota being estimated to equal one-third
of the average crop per acre raised by labour
under direct taxation. And every group was
constituted a 44 mutual responsibility ” sub-gang.
That is to say, for the misdeeds of any one
member of a group, the other members would
suffer.
The group tenanting the hut on Section 5 B was
constituted by some 4 4 old hands’’whose records
were of the very 44 best. ”+ Tested by the official
standards of the elder System, they would have
been welcomed by Lucifer with a 44 Hail, fellows,
well met! ” And thereupon the soft-hearted
Commandant resolved they should have a chance
to reform. Very much to the amusement of the
Commissariat and other officials did he announce
this determination.
The sub-gangers were five in number. Osborne,
of whom we dare say no more than that it was a
daily wonder to his comrades how he escaped
hanging; Peake, a small-skulled, thirty-year old
lump of a physical deformity that rivalled his
moral nature ; a gentlemanly ex-forger who was
popularly known as 44 Barrington ” from the
circumstance that he knew off by heart the account
of that immortal scoundrel’s career; a“Swinger”+—
Felix—less sullen than brutish in feature, and
of gigantic physical strength; and Reynell, a
former soldier of the 44 Fighting Half-Hundred,”
who had been transported to Norfolk Island by a
V.D.L. military court for desertion to Maori-
land in a whaler.
A tall, strapping fellow, who carried himself
with military erectness, was Reynell, and it was
his boast that his parchment record was as long as
himself. Of course, the assertion was a slight
exaggeration, but Reynell was given to little
whimsicalities. A devil when roused, he was, as
a rule, a merry soul, who was pleasantly cynical.
He entered into crime with the same zest
as into a battle-square. “It was but putting
the bayonet into the law instead of the enemy,”
he said to Mr. Pery, Superintendent of Agricul-
ture, one day when Pery asked him why he would
persist in settintr the authorities at defiance. “ I
have to let the devil out of me somehow', sir, and
as Her Gracious Mejesty —God bless her ! —won’t
employ me against her enemies, I have to make
enemies of my own. And the law’s a grand enemy
to fight, sir ! It’ll take such a lot of beating !”
Against a criminal of this temper the Law had
used everything in its dread armoury, except
44 the spread-eagle,” the gallows, and—a kind
word and good faith. These last two instruments
of unusual punishment Captain Maconochie now
determined to supply. He appointed Reynell
leader of 5 B sub-gang at a Sunday morning
“ after chapel ” muster at the Iron Room.
“ Henry Reynell, per Coquette, colonial trans-
port,” called the mustering-overseer,
“Reynell, how do you come to be included
among 4 old hands ’ when you’re a 4 colonial ’ ?”
questioned the Commandant, with his pleasant
resonance of voice, as soon as the man had replied
with his 44 Here !”
4 4 The honourable Court gave me 14 years, y’r
honor, and as I didn’t think it enough I got ’em
to make it life?” There was a ripple of laughter
in the ranks.
44 How ?” asked the Captain, who took no notice
of the demonstration.
44 1 struck the corporal of the court guard—and
so the honourable Court gave me what I wanted.”
44 Not the same court ? It could not act at once
and without being formally convened by the
Colonel-Commanding ?”
44 As to that, sir, I can’t say. All I know is
that same Court convicted me—and I came here
with a double sentence. Therefore, Major Ryan
thought me entitled, sir, to all the emoluments,
rights and privileges of an old hand. I’ve been in
irons all the time I’ve been here.”
44 Will you take a word of advice from me,
Reynell?” said the Commandant.
44 Will —I—what, y’r honor ?” asked Reynell,
with a choking breath in his voice that might
have been amusement, or might have been sheer
amazement at the autocrat of a penal-settle-
ment assuming so extraordinary a tone.
44 Take my advice, my good fellow, just to drop
that sneering manner of speech.” There was
a genuine kindness in the words. Reynell drew
himself up to his full height and clenched his fist.
Those who stood by thought he was about to
transform recklessness of tongue into madness of
action. The line between a murderer and a hero
is often but a hair’s breadth, and this man, who
might easily become a hero, might as easily pass
the line.
However, other answer than this he did not
make. He flung out his closed hand and said :
44 So easy to preach, y’r honor ! With the iron in
the soul, and the cat on the back, and the
bayonet-point in the body, what wonder the
sneer’s on the lip? So easy for you gentlemen to
deal with heartless numbers. You say, Numbers
37-189 —that’s me-and 39-204—that’s Felix —
don’t feel, God above —don’t we! And what
weapon ha’ we to fight the System with if you
won’t allow us to use our tongues? Even at our
peril we must use ’em !”
He stopped and gathered strength for a last
phrase which quivered with the under-thrill of his
bitterness. 44 It’s fighting that’s our last hope of
keeping something of manhood to ourselves, sir !
Fight!—l’d die if I did not fight—die or go mad !”
Outbursts of this sort were common enough
among the more intelligent convicts, but
Maconochie never ceased to be impressed by
them. The receptive sympathy of the man—
which proved his ruin as an administrator —was
always stirred when the note of strength and
sincerity ran through the transport’s utterances.
He listened now to Reynell with a patience that
to his under-strappers and to the felons at muster
seemed at once wonderful and childish.
With mutual nods and winks (in hearty enjoy-
ment of the joke) the gentlemen of the Commis-
sariat who accompanied him listened to his
ludicrously feeble reception of Convict Reynell’s
attack on the System’s amenities.
44 Reynell,” he stepping a pace nearer to
the ranks as he spoke, “ I am going to trust you—
I will give you a farm—you and any four others
you may choose to pick out of the Old Hands ! ”
“ You —are—not jollying—me, your Honour ?”
“ No—you will find by-and-bye that I never
4 jolly! ’”
“ Then, by G —, sir, I’ll be true man to you ! ’
From the rank of men from which Reynell had
been called out came in two or three distinct voices
a shout of :
“ The Ring’ll see ’bout that ! The Oath ! The
Oath! ”
» * *
Reynell, instantly flushed with the strenuous
hope that had been created by Maconochie’s
words, paled as instantly. Then :
“ I’ll take it back, y’r Honor. I’ll remain as I
am—a ‘ good ’ man !”
“That’s right, that’s right, my man !” rejoined
the Commandant, genially. Again, a sibillant
chorus from the ranks. The transports were
tickled agreeably at what they thought his mis-
apprehension. They had understood Reynell.
Reynell, they knew, was simply adopting the
vocabulary of the damned, in which “darkness
was light and light darkness.” But the laughter
stopped instantly as Maconochie raised his hand
—and did the fatalest thing of his commandancy.
“Men!” he exclaimed, “No more of that!
And now listen!”
A rubbing and clinking of irons and a shuffling
of feet rose on the calm air as the men settled
themselves into position. They had heard they
had got a “bad” preaching Commandant—and
now Fate was about to confirm the report by
currsing them with a second sermon on the one
day.
“Men, listen ! A threat has been used about
the‘Ring.’ Now, I tell you—‘Ring’ members
and non-members of the ‘Ring’—that 1 am
resolved to crush that society out of existence. ”
From among the massed men a confused clamour
arose. “So other Confidants have said—and they
failed!” “ Better not try !” “ Ye’ll ha’to croak
first! ” A chorus of defiance in which rumbled an
accent of triumph. The System for three genera-
tions of Islanders had been trying to kill the
“Ring” and the “Ring” was still immutable
and impregnable. The men who were of the
“Ring” feared its despotism, but gloried in its
traditions and its power. The men whose names
were not scored in its mysteriously-kept roll,
respected it and admired it, for was it not a rock
that withstood the shocks of the Authorities? —an
empire supreme over an empire otherwise omni-
potent ?
Now, Maconochie had meant to say that the
only uprooting force he intended to apply to the
“ Ring ” was that of kindness and justice. His
wish was to render the Secret Society innocuous
by depriving it of any occasion for the exercise of
its undoubtedly enormous capacity for desperate
action. But he was given no chance of explaining
himself. Though they thought that the loss of
their Sunday dinner—deeply cherished treat! —
was involved in the uproar, the 150 men, moved
by a common impulse of passion which, like a
tornado-wave, swept all before it, continued and
increased their clamour. They shouted, whistled,
clanked their irons. Every sound was an inflec-
tion of evil. To the officials inured by years of
familiarity with the Island life to such demonstra-
tions, there was nothing particularly alarming—
certainly nothing distressing in the storm. To the
Commandant, however, sensitive in feeling,
exalted in imagination, and subject to a curious
persistence of reasoning which convinced him
* The constitution of “The Ring,” so far as it has been
ascertained, will be described in the course of the sequel
to this story. Here it may be briefly explained that
“ The Ring," as originally organised, and till the epoch
of Major Childs—Maconochie’s successor—embraced four
“ Circles ” and a Head—the circle of “ Three ” members,
the circle of “ Five,” the circle of “ Seven,” and the circle
of “Nine,” or a total initiate membership of twenty-five.
In the slang of “The Ring”—a distinct species of the
“Flash ” language-the “Head”—in this story termed
the “ One was accorded a term awfully blasphemous.
So terrible was the fear inspired by “The Ring” that the
memory of it made old “Black Norfolkers” reticent 30
years after the society was virtually broken up by the
abandonment of the Island as a penal settlement; and no
department of the author’s researches has caused him
greater trouble, and presented him with comparatively
smaller results, than the history of this organisation.
t At Norfolk Island it was the awful custom among the
more hardened convicts to invert the meaning of “ good”
and “bad.” A “good man ” was a notorious criminal; a
“bad” one was a man who sought to act honestly and
purely.

every transport was less an offender against society
than a victim of society’s errors and stupidities,
the noise was a literal shock.
He held his band up to command silence. A
strong hiss from the centre greeted the gesture.
He folded his arms, as though to wait patiently
for the cessation of the tumult. The challenge
was responded to by shrieks of laughter.
He lifted his cap and passed his handkerchief
across his forehead. Fifty hands derisively copied
the action. It was an admission that he was
beaten, and they delighted in it as their nostrils
would have done in the scent of roast meat.
He turned his back upon men, and
motioned to a gaol-warder. Assistant-Deputy-
Commissariat. General Shanks thought he pur-
posed to order up the main-guard, and for the first
time was prepared to confess to himself that the
Commandant was something more than a dreamer.
And —Mr. Shanks was to be disappointed.
Instead of bringing up at the “double ” a file of
twelve men—instead of issuing in bloody sequence,
incisive commands : “Ready! Present! Fire!”
Captain Maconochie had sent to his own stores
for, —tobacco ! The imbecility of that act! —how
it startled Mr. Shanks! How it spoilt his
Sunday’s dinner and compelled him to sacrifice
his afternoon nap so that he might write to
Governor Gipps and Mr. E. Deas-Thomson !
“ Tobacco for rebels ! The establishment is
going to the devil!” he groaned later to Mrs.
Shanks. “ Tobacco ! —when they should have had
lead. If he had made requisition upon me, and
not have drawn from his own store, I’d have
refused there and then !” For Mr. Shanks’ heart
was sore within him.
As for the gentry of the Iron Room, their
turbulence held till the box of tobacco was placed
at the Commandant’s feet. And then it faded,
with a final hiss and splatter as a breaking wave
dies against the shingle. They were stupefied at
this unique form of punishment.
“ ’E’s a-goin’ to ’cap coals o’ fire on our ’eads !”
exclaimed someone, but the remark passed un-
heeded. The mass were too surprised even for
ribald comments.
* # *
“Reynell !” Maconochie called.
Reynell looked round before answering. Did
the “ Ring ” raise objection ? If it did, there was
no visible or audible sign of its refusal. And he
stepped forward ; and, to the Commandant’s
pleasure, saluted.
“ Reynell, call out four men to assist you !”
“ What for, sir?”
“To distribute that tobacco—half a fig to a
man.”
Reynell stared at the Captain—then gazed back
at the massed men. For guidance—for a hint as
to whether he dare take the bribe on their behalf ?
Most likely so. And so near is weak kindness to
refined cruelty, there was notone man there in those
ranks of ironed, yellow, brown-and-grey garbed
felons, with the symbols of shame on their bodies
and the glare of the human beast in their eyes,
but hated Maconochie in that moment of ordeal.
Not a man there but would have risked severe
penalties to obtain a fraction of a fig; scarcely a
score of the one hundred and fifty but what had
already gone through the mire of humiliation fora
“ bit.” Therefore their hearts beat with a venom-
ous strength—because he tempted them sorely.
For why did they not answer to Reynell’s un-
spoken enquiry ?
Those who were not of the “ Ring ” dare not
speak. In collective action the “Ring” led the
“ private ” convicts.
And those who were of the Society grew weak
with the temptation. But then—to accept it, was
to acknowledge Maconochie’s supremacy, and to
confess a defeat.
For half a minute the two parties stood silent.
The surf, half a mile away, drove its monotone
over the still air. From the wooded sides of
Mount Pitt, on the other hand, travelled, not un-
melodiously, the screech of parrots. A wedge-
tailed eagle poised majestically over the square,
and hoarsely flung a taunt to the imprisoned
creatures. Save for these sounds, the parade was
as silent as is the lull before the revolt of thunder
against its confines.'
Then —first one sharp “No!”—next, two or
three were joined in the repetition—and finally, in
impetuous volume, the fierce negatives rolled from
the ranks. Never did monk of the desert make so
great a renunciation! In that volleyed mono-
syllable those outcasts of civilisation refused a
pleasure for which, under other circumstances,
they would have gleefully bartered their souls.
“No!” A brazen, defiant “No!” which
epitomised the curses of Tophet.
Reynell marched back to his place; and
Maconochie knew, as the storm of curses and
cheers burst out again, that the “Ring’’was
triumphant.
# # *
Unless
Commissariat-Officer Shanks suggested, with a
semi-sneer, the application of old methods.
“Try, Captain Maconochie, a platoon! There’s
pretty considerable of a quietening influence in a
volley, sir I That is mutiny, and if you don’t get
the better of them now, they’ll have every iron
off their ankles in an hour, and then you’ll have
to shoot the lot, unless you want us all killed.”
“ No !” replied the Commandant. “Ball-cart-
ridge is the last thing I propose to use on society’s
wrecks. Mr. Gaoler— this thing has gone far
enough. Finish the muster and give ’em their
dinners!”
“ What, sir ! Their dinners !” Really, the
gaoler was to be excused for his patent astonish-
ment.
“ Ay,—the poor fellows shan’t suffer for my
blunder in tactics. The mistake was mine—l’ve
taken ’em the wrong way to-day.”
And with this remark, so subversive of all the
conventions and principles of the System, for
whenever before did a penal commandant admit
he was in error, Captain Maconochie touched
his cap, in graceful acknowledgment of the salute
of his subordinates and left the muster-yard,
# • *
The whole of the Iron Room transports
enjoyed their dinners the more for the sauce of
triumph. For dessert they were gratified by
another delicious morsel.
The Commandant sent an order to the Gaoler
to despatch by G. 30 a.m. on Monday Henry Rey-
nell, per Coquette (Colonial), and “four other
men that the said prisoner should nominate,” to
Farm 5 8., therein to be installed as “sub-gang in
charge.” The proceeding was, it is needless to
say, altogether exceptional. But then the Island
owned an altogether exceptional commander, and
it had proved a day of exceptional occurrences.
And it was, doubtless, in accordance with the
spirit of the joke that the Gaoler, as he communi-
cated the decision to Reynell, mocked him by
doffing his cabbage-tree, and addressed him with a
scoffing irony.
“Would it please Mr. Reynell to nominate
the gentlemen who were to accompany him ?”
Reynell took the jest admirably. He craved
five minutes to make his selection, and within
that time had informed the officer that Osborne,
Peake, Barrington, and “Swinger” Felix would
form his comradeship. For the committee of the
“Ring” had raised no objection. “’Twarn’t
going out to the farmsteads, Reynell,” said a high
ruler of the league—a Three —“that we complain
of, but your promise to be a true man ! No chap
in the fellowship shall go ‘ bad ’ without permis-
sion. It’s breaking oath !”
And consent being thus obtained—we translate
the “flash” language habitually employed in
“Ring” business—the choice was, as we have
said, made and 5 B group constituted.
On Monday, when the dormitories turned out
at 5.30, the first thing done by the new sub-gang
was to present themselves at the “ blacksmith’s
shop ” and have the rivets driven from the bazils.
Felix was last at the low anvil. As the bazil of
his left leg fell to the ground he expanded the
massive brawniness of his chest with long draughts
of tonic morning air ; and then clutched Reynell
with a wrestler’s grasp.
“Why, lad, I be tha’ man for ever an’ a day.
I never ’ud ha’ got rid o’ them damned clinks but
for thee until the day I wed the worms. Bill
Felix is tha’ man for ever an’ a day !”
And now let us gather up the links of the
story.
Monday was formal court-day, and, therefore,
none of the group siw the Commandant till the
evening. Muster had passed over —the mutual
responsibility farms were mustered only by their
leader, he answering for his group—and the men
were busy preparing their “tea,” and rejoicing
in the novel sensation of was virtual free-
dom, when the Captain walked up to the hub.
They ceased their preparations and saluted.
The spontaneity of the movement was plain, and
it thrilled the “old man’s” heart to notice it.
Something, he thought, of that voluntary respect
for just authority, which is an accompaniment of
manhood, had been generated in the men by that
one day’s liberty, and surely his experiment was
about to be justified ? He smiled gaily as he re-
turned the salute.
“Now, men,” he said, “don’t mind me! Get
on with your tea —I am sure you must need it
after all this day in the fields ! ”
In forty years of the System never had Osborne
heard the like. Ho bent his eyes to the block of
stone which did duty as a temperary table, and
fumbled with his tin maize-meal dish. The
others, with the exception of Peake, were also
affected; Reynell to the point of turning his head
away so that neither the Captain nor his group-
mates should discern the tear that scalded his
cheek.
“■Men!” continued the Commandant —ever
deeper touched by evidences of gratitude than by
testimonies of insult, “I wish you would trust
me ! I want to be a friend to you—-to every man
of the 1000 souls in prison here I Come, sirs, forget
I’m the Commandant—the ‘old man.’ Think of
me, for the time being at all events, as a man—
one who deeply sympathises with your sufferings
and who will only be too glad to alleviate them in
every way he can without violating his duty to
those who sent him here! Come, what do you
say ?”
Peake was the first to speak. “Reynell, y’r
Honour, is our leader !” A dogged resistance to
any softening influence was easily to be under-
stood from his manner.
“Then, Reynell,-speak for yourself at least —for
the others if you can.”
The e.vsoldier drew his under-lip in, and bit it
till blood came, in the severity of the struggl a
between the Past and the Future that might be
Then he gulped rather than uttered his answer. ’
“I take back—the insult—of - yesterday sir
I’ll be true man to you—so help me God ! ’And
the ‘ Ring ’ may do its worst.”
Maconochie knew that, come what might-
disdain from Privy Councillors and Secretaries of
State, cold water from Governors, and sneers and
insubordination from smaller officials—yet still he
had plucked one soul from the pit. After a few
more words of friendly tenor he returned to
Government House.
* * *
Upon his going, Peake dropped his thin
mask of hypocrisy and looked what he was— the
child of the devil his father, and the System his
mother. Other parentage had he known none
When, as a hunchbacked boy of eight, he first
understood a little of the meaning of life, the
System was already nourishing him at her breasts.
And because of this must we excuse him some-
what.
Peake, when the Captain’s steps could not be
longer heard, pulled off his waist-strap.
“Hold!” He threw out an end to “Penman
Barrington. ” • ‘ Barrington ” paled—but grasped
the leather.
“ Reynell, you sneakin’ cull —come here !”
And Reynell, too, obeyed the strange command.
He took the other end. The strap was pulled
taut. Then Osborne and Felix each laid hold
upon it in the centre, standing on either side of
it. The four thus formed a cross. Sometimes in
the cross of the “Ring,” the hands touched and
clasped ; but never in the cross of denunciation.
As this was.
Three—five-seven times Peake walked round
the group ; and as he moved he recited the
Convict Oath.
At last, he stopped suddenly—at the end of the
third repetition.
“ Osborne, you’re a ‘Sevener ?’ ”
Osborne, hoarse with suppressed fear, muttered
“Yes.”
“ And you’re a Sevener, accused?” Reynell
was thus addressed. He nodded assent.
“ What are you, Bill?”
“ A Kiner !” answered Felix.
“ Barrington ?”
“ A Fiver !”
“ And I’m a Three. We’re all denominations.
All denominations necessary to convene when it’s
a Sevener as is to go up before . Do any
object?”
Silence. Only Reynell shuddered.
‘‘Then, the Niner shall bid the Niners, and
the Sevener the Seveners, and the Fiver the
Fivers, and the Three the Threes to “Ring” lodge
on Sabbath next if the One ratifies, and the busi-
ness shall be to try Sevener Henry Reynell, for
that he played our noble society false, and pro-
mised to be true man to an Establishment officer
and defies the society ! So the Devil help you
all!”
And some trembling lips muttered alow “So
the Devil help us !”
# * #
This was the manner of convening the
“Ring.” And the account of the “Ring’s”
session to try Reynell, per Coquette, we must tell
you another time.

The Double Cross.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
Now my mate and myself we were low
In the funds, and were racing for lucre,
And the Crow was the best of our two,
But we knew
That the coin of the public would go
On to him, so we “ played for a euchre.”
We fixed that the mare was to win,
And, of course, we should back her like water,
At the last we’d get twenties to one;
’Twould be fun,
When the public had piled all their tin
On the Crow, just like lambs for the slaughter.
So I left all the bets to my mate,
While I went off to see to the rider,
And I heard the “ books ” calling the odds,
And, ye gods,
The mare was made favourite straight,
And the Crow was the rankest outsider.
I can tell you it puzzled me much,
As I walked on the lawn, after dinner,
For how came the public to know
That the Crow
Was to run just as stiff as a crutch
While the little bay mare was a spinner ?
Till a party came by and he told
What was up, after backing and filling,
“Your mate’s thrown you over,” said he,
“ And you’ll see,
When the mare wins her race you’ll be sold,
And you won’t get as much as a shilling.”
So I ordered the boy on the Crow,
He must win or I’d give him a hiding,
I backed him for all I could get,
And, you bet,
That my mate and his crowd felt a blow
When they saw how the youngster was riding.
For the cheers and the shouting were loud
When our pair reached the turn, racing level,
But the Crow came away in the straight,
And my mate
Came to me with a blustering crowd,
And I told him to go to the devil.
And the fight that ensued after that,
Well, it proved to those jokers so clever,
That the man whom they thought was a mug
As a“pug”
Was the clwilpion of old Lambing Flat,
And the best of his weight on the River.
And racing, folks think, ought to be
For encouraging nags that are gallant,
But they’re nowadays soon disabused,
For it’s used
For maintaining of jokers like me,
Who subscribes myself,
ONE OF THE TALENT.
In Morocco, the Moor found drunk by the
police is simply chained up to a wall until he gets
sober. Good idea to chain up “ drunks” publicly.
The Emperor of Austria would not allow
Rosalie Schneider, the murderess, to be executed.
No woman has been executed in Austria since
1808. If Austria can abolish woman-execution
she can also get along without executing men]

of having a classical (or encyclopaedic) education :
Among the most noticeable of the necrologies com-

Apropos Walter Bradley's now historic
dictum that “a successful auctioneer must drink.”
One of Sydney’s most successful auctioneers is
almost a total abstainer, but he “gets there all
the same ” —by arranging at hotels to be supplied
out of whisky-bottles containing 95 per cent,
water. By the time lie re-waters his grog it is as
weak as a speck of a “high” homoeopathic
trituration in a ship’s tank.
Queen Victoria lately presented an ele-
phant, as a special mark of esteem, to her royal
friend, the Sultan of Morocco. The Sultan is
fond of that elephant, and uses it instead of a
hangman. Nearly every day he has some two or
three criminals brought, before him, and laid flat
on the ground, and then the elephant gently sits
down on each one in succession, and there is a
squashy sound which answers, in that primitive
laud, instead of a certificate of death. When the
proceedings are over the bloodstains are washed
off the back part of the animal with a hose,
and Queen Victoria’s present is stowed away for
future service.
Writes a Tasmanian correspondent: —
Dear Bulletin, “General” Booth’s colonisation
scheme is being forestalled, at least so far as Tasmania is
concerned. A month ago a batch of 30 immigrants were
landed at Davenport, on the N.W. coast, were given
grants of land by the Government, and started extensive
saw-milling works. Not satisfied with this, they procured
a vessel and, after exploring Bass’ Straits, they visited
King’s Island, and returning to Tasmania were imme-
diately granted 10,000 acres of land. This means that a
splendid piece of timber-country, and valuable timber at
that, is to be entirely under the control of the immigrants
(who, it may be mentioned, do not employ outside
labour, and imported every article they required, even to
a saucepan), while hundreds of necessitous families are to
be seen on all sides. It will be said that the Tasmanians
should have taken up the land first, but they did not
know a word about it being free. There are living on
King’s Island (farming and timber-cutting) 300 or 400
families, but the land must be given up to the English
immigrants because they are “enterprising,” at least
that is the excuse put forward.— C. A. Lake.
Sometime since a map-publisher in one of
the Australias, received from the Education
Department a lot of maps of the World to be
mounted on cloth. He handed them over to one
of his men, an American, who in cutting up the
calico made the pieces too small. To get out of
the difficulty the Yank sliced a strip out of the
middle of each map, cutting out the British Isles,
Portugal, and a considerable portion of the
Atlantic. When the map publisher saw the
result he was flabbergasted. “ Good Heavens,
man” he said to the Yank “what have you done?”
“What have I done?” said the American coolly,
“ why I cut the calico too small, so I sliced a piece
out of the middle of the maps—you see I’ve only
cut out England and a few other little places
like that!” [lnteresting lie. — Ed. B.]

FEDERATION OR NO FEDERATION.
The latest scheme for Federation
Claims to be for the country’s weal,
While all may prove but botheration,
Which can’t be said of Pemkll’s Meal.
His Golden Meal is what I mean,
Tis good and pure and cheap I ween.
Kauri-gum digging is a staple industry in
the Auckland district (M.L.). From 7000 to 10,000
men are employed, and the export frequently
reaches ,£500,000 worth annually. The gum is
worth about £40 “at the stump,” that is in the
bush, but on reaching the store it is sorted into
about 20 different qualities, ranging from “ scrap-
ings ” worth £7 a ton (used in the manufacture of
fire-kindlers), up to “firsts,” worth £200 a ton.
The equipment of the digger is a long iron spear
and a spade. The ground in an old kauri forest
is prodded with the spear, and when a hard sub-
stance is struck the spade is brought into inquisi-
tion. The experienced digger has “the pull”
over the new-chum in the spearing business. He
can tell by tasting the point of his spear if he has
touched gum, whereas the novice may dig for a
couple of hours and find but a root or a
stone as the result of his quest. The gum is found
in small patches ranging from a few pounds up
to a couple of ewt. There is reason to believe
that most of the “ copal ” varnish now used
throughout the world is manufactured from
Maoriland kauri-gum. Gum-digging is the refuge
of the indigent of every class.
The British pioneer settlers in Yokohama
had their modesty shocked by finding that male
and female Japs, bathed together without any
water costumes. Their protests worried the poor
Jans., who were anxious to please. After a hard
“ think,” a genius was struck by an inspiration
which was at once adopted. The Japs, divided
their bathing-places into two compartments, and
set one apart for males and the other for females.
The division was effected by stretching a rope
lengthwise, the centre above the water. Then
they called the British in to come and rejoice with
them about their moral x-eformation.

The writer of “Fugitive Notes” in the
S.M. Herald attempts to refute the vegetarians’
assertion that the Jews, as a carnivorous race,
were cruel, but “A. B. C.” cannot have studied
his Old Testament with much profit if it is Ins
honest belief that the Chosen People were not as
cruel as they were aggressive and bloodthirsty.
Let him read their undoubtedly authentic history
after the exodus, of their unprovoked invasions,
their slayings, burnings, and the peculiar trophies
of victory which they selected. Let him read Ins
Gibbon, and find how they behaved in Caesarea
under the then gentle sway of the Romans. Th e
Egyptians were a humane and moral people by
comparison, and when they sought, like the Czar’
to deal with what was to them a Chinese Question'
they merely pursued a policy of repression, it
might pay the S.M.H. to reproduce the Bible in
serial form. We fear, however, that Editor
Curnow would mar its pristine terseness, and
darken understanding. He would describe Mrs.
Rahab, for instance, as “a person well known to
the Jericho police,” Judah as an evidently
respectable and high-minded gentleman who was
alleged ” to have “ misbehaved himself in Meso-
potamia Park,” and Absalom’s singular perform-
ance on the roof in sight of all Israel as “the un-
fettered impulse of a self-willed mind breaking
through the tyrannous bonds of conventionality.”
Padding like this would only enable the rising
generation to get as far as the Captivity, es-
pecially if tho Rbv. Editor handed the battle
pieces to the military importer and the horsey
incidents about Jehu and Ahab to the unre-
generate Rowley Pickering.

A British pauper s funeral was in progress
the other day at Droxford (Eng.). The deceased
was being shoved along in a species of hand-cart
with his wife and daughters, and his friends— if
he had any—walking behind. When the proces-
sion was about halfway to the cemetery, the
coffin, being a slop-made article supplied by the
parish, went to pieces, and the dead man rolled
out into the road. The only thing he had on was
a pair of socks. His unexpected appearance
caused a good deal of excitement, but the parish
authorities hurriedly put him back on the cart
and nailed the broken coffin together again, and
the procession went on its way.
“WHY HE NEVER LOOKED BEHIND
KIM ”
“ After this I never looked behind me.”
This is a very common expression. What do
people mean by it ? Lot’s wife looked behind her
and was changed into a pillar of salt. A locomo-
tive driver in America looked behind him one day
last summer and so didn’t see an open drawback
in front of him. Hence a wreck and great loss of
life. A man in London failed to look behind him
and was run down by a hansom. What shall wc
do as a rule ? Look behind us or not ?
We introduce a man who says he never looked
behind him—after a certain time. How are we la
take his meaning ? Why, by letting him explain
it.
He goes on to say that in one day in February
1890, he was suddenly seized with dizziness ami
pain in his head. Like all healthy people, under
similar circumstances, he didn’t know what tu
make of it. He says he felt strange and queer he
shivered as though the weather had suddenly
turned cold, and then flushed with the heat a:;
though it had turned hot again. What ailed him;
His doctor said he was attacked with influenza
and ordered him to bed. He went to bed. A few
days later the fever left him, but the illness did
not. it merely assumed another form. Ri s
tongue looked like a piece of brown leather, and
his skin and the whites of his eyes became yellow
like old parchment. Wo must all eat to live, but
when this man tried to eat, the food went against
him, and after he had swallowed it by main force
it caused such pain in the chest, aide, and stomach
that he wished ho had let it alone. Then his
heart began to palpitate, and ho says he felt low,
languid, and tired. He had what he calls a sink-
ing feeling at the pit of the stomach and a
craving which nothing satisfied.
Being unable to take any but liquid food he
grew so weak that he was barely able to walk.
Then his heart troubled him once more, and, to
quote his own words, “ As I sat in my chair 1
could hear my heart thumping as if somebody was
pounding me on the back.”
This showed that the heart had too much work
on hand and was struggling under it like a horse
trying to carry two men. “ I got very little sleep
at night,” he says, “and would lie awake for
hours tossing about on the bed.” This sort of
thing is very wearing, and we are not surprised to
learn that he lost flesh until little was left of him
but skin and bone. “My cheeks,” he says, “sank
in until they were almost drawn together, and
people shook their heads and predicted that my
time in this world was nearly up. Still I had all
confidence in my physician and kept on taking his
medicine. From first to last I took some forty or
fifty bottles of it (of all kinds) without benefit.
“Finally one day the doctor sounded my lungs
and asked me if any of our family died of con-
sumption. He said that the heart palpitation was
caused by dyspepsia. Then he said I had better
take further advice; he could do no more for me.
This was after nine months of his treatment. I
gave up all hopes of getting better, and, indeed,
no one expected me to.
“It was now winter again, December, 1890,
One day I found a little book or pamphlet in the
house, that I had never seen before. It was about
a medicine called Mother Seigel’s Syrup, and
described a case like mine having been cured by it.
Without going into all my hopes and fears on the
point, it is enough to say that I got a bottle from
Mr. Kirkmarx, Chemist, Ellerby Lane. I took the
contents of that bottle and certainly felt a little
better. I took a second and began to eat solid
food, which agreed with me. ||||
“ After this 1 never looked behind me, though
my recovery was a work of time, for I was very
much reduced. I stuck to the medicine, and with
good reason, and at last got back to my work,
strong and well, and have remained so ever since.
When I went back to the works the foreman and
others gathered round me and asked what had
wrought the wonderful change. I answered,
“Mother Seigel’s Syrup had wrought it.” When
I said I wished to start work they told me I must
first be examined by a doctor. The doctor said 1
was fit for work and I went to work the next
morning and have never lost a minute since.
“ I wish others to know what Seigel’s Syrup has
done for me, and I give the proprietors permission
to publish this brief account of my case. I am a
cloth presser by trade and have worked at Messrs.
Hepworth and Sons, Clay Pit Lane, for four
years.” Harvey Askew, 2 Back Timber Place,
Ellerby Lane, Leeds.
The doctor was right in saying that the apparom
heart trouble in Mr. Askew’s case arose from
dyspepsia, for dyspepsia was his only ailment.
And if he had used Mother Seigel’s Syrup m
February, 1890, he would have had no tale to tell,
for he would have been all right directly. Asi
is we are glad that after he did try it he had no
relapse. He never looked behind him.
